      \n SPONTANEITY, part one
      There will be longer quotations here because you have to understand the whole chain of thoughts. I will therefor break this chapter into two posts.

      p. 85Most schools encourage children to not show creativity and ingenuity. Research has up to this point shown that creative children are disliked by their teachers. Torrance gives an eyewitness of an "exceptional creative boy" who questioned on of the rules in the textbook. "The teacher became furious, even in the presence of the principle. She boiled of anger: 'So! You think you know more than this book!' ..." (E P Torrance, Guiding Creative Talent, Prentice-Hall, 1962)
      /.../
      One of my students spent two years in a classroom, where the teacher had hung a big sign over the black board. It said "Your attitude should be 'Yes, teacher!'".
      The first rule of roleplaying:
      1. The game master is always right.
      The second rule of roleplaying:
      2. If the game master isn't right, see rule no 1.

      In the first chapter, Johnstone wrote: "When I grew up, the world started to become gray and boring. /.../ I thought a diminish of perception came with age /.../ I didn't realize, that the ability to experience clear and intensive is a matter of mind.", and it's the same thing with spontaneity. We learn as we grow up to adopt to others\u2014to not stand out. This is a sort of self-censoring that we need to break down in order to become creative in roleplaying games.

      When I read about the teacher and how she treated the creative boy, I remember a conversation I had with a game master that were so annoyed when the players didn't do what he expected. This, and rule 1 and 2, is an attitude that chokes creativity in roleplaying games.

      I usually say that to be able to play This Is Pulp or InSpectres, with me as a game master, it takes experienced roleplaying gamers 20 minutes and non-gamers 10 minutes to understand how to play the games. This is because I have to break down and rebuild the attitude that the experienced roleplayers have, in order to make them more creative and spontaneous.

      p. 90-91It may seem like humans aren't that creative, but they are extremely ingenious when it comes to rationalize their own actions. /.../ A friend of mine got discovered buck naked in a closet, of a furious husband. The wife screamed: "I have never seen that man before in my life!" "I must be on the wrong floor", said my friend. None of these explanations are satisfactory, /.../ but they came automatically.

      Sometimes I shock my students that has be taught by extremely orthodox "method" teachers.
      "Be sad", I say.
      "What do you mean, be sad?"
      "Just be said. See what happens."
      "But what is my motivation?"
      /.../
      "That's not in particular sad. You're only pretending."
      "But you asked to me pretend."
      "Raise one arm. Well, why did you raise it?"
      "Because you asked me."
      "Yes, but why would you had raised it?"
      "To get a hold of a strap, on the subway."
      Say the first thing that comes into mind, because that is usually the best idea. I would never had thought of a subway as a reason of why I raised my hand. Don't hesitate, that's what being spontaneous is about. Thinking that it's too ordinary? Obviously not. Your own thoughts are ordinary to you, but they will almost always seem unique to me. Even if they aren't unique, it's what I will expect, and you fulfilling that expectation can also be a good thing. I will talk more about this in the next chapter called Narrating With Theater.

      p. 103"There are humans that prefer to say "Yes and there are humans that prefer to say "No". Those who says "Yes will be rewarded through the experience of adventures, and those who says "No" are rewarded through the safety and comfort that they achieve. There are a lot more "Naysayers" than "Yeasayers", but you can train one to be another. ("Yeasayers and Naysayers", Journal of Abnormal and Social Psychology, Vol 160, No 2, 1960.)
      /.../
      There is a connection here to status play, because low status players tend to accept and high status players tend to block. They block every transaction, if they feel that they can't control it.
      What does "rolling the die" means? What does it mean to have a game master with unlimited power? What does it mean to have game mechanics, that the game master can enforce, to limits what the players can do?

      When I playtested The Murder of Mr. Crow, a game where you aren't allowed to say "no", some people said that they wanted to sometimes be able to say "no". If we assume that the game has a working rule set for interacting with each other, what does that tell you about them?

      It's my belief that the game master needs to let go and start accepting the other participants' ideas. I'm not saying that we should get rid of the rule mechanics, because that will leave the game master empty handed, but what I have been talking about all through this thread is the use of structures in play. Structures can come in form of game mechanics or techniques. I also been talking about how there is no such thing as improvisation but instead the use of (unspoken) structures. Thirdly, I also mentioned that the structures are there to make people communicate\u2014to make them interact with each other.

      So try to create an interaction through saying "Yes", by taking what others have contributed with and give it a spin. Also, when someone feel that they are acknowledged, then they will feel good about themselves. So what this means is not only a fruitful interaction model that frees spontaneity and creativity but also a reward system. And if I say something, you reply, and I adopt to it and reply, then we got this circular feedback system that brings both investment and immersion/presence/flow.

      ---

      Why I'm quoting from this book isn't because it's a sort of Bible that everyone should listen to, but because here are ideas that I had for the last five years about roleplaying games. What Keith Johnstone writes corresponds so perfectly with my own ideas, that he's actually verbalizing them.

      The next part will be about blocking and accepting, and the huge difference between "Yes, but" in roleplaying games and improv, even though roleplaying gamers got that technique from improvisation theater in the first place.

      \n\nRickard \n\n\n

      \n\nJune 2014 \n edited July 2014
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n There are some contradictions in what I wrote above.

      People are afraid to stand out, but they also want to provide original ideas. The original ideas are often the dull ones, because the ordinary ideas are something everybody can give a response to. If I explained Hitlers return through a metaphysical aspect that involved the Illuminati, I would exclude a lot of people from adding ideas to it, compared to if I said "Hitler fled". How did Hitler flee? That's up to you to add.

      I and @Hasimir talked about the the power of saying no a few months back. While it may seem like I despise the use of "no", it can come in handy sometimes. Hasimir talked about his veto rule and how it was used to make people be on the same wave length. If someone didn't like what another participant contributed with, that person could use the veto and then explain why the rule was used. The first person could then adopt to it and change it's contribution. (Again with the loop.)

      I've noticed how "Yes, but" can bring comfort when I game master InSpectres or This Is Pulp. Not so much for me, but for my players when I say that they can invent anything in the environment as long as they leave room for me to fill in with "Yes, but". If I don't introduce "Yes, but"\u2014a technique that can negate what they came up with\u2014it can otherwise leave the players uncertain in what they can fill out the environment with.

      Another thing that brings comfort in playing is the use of the X card. If we're about to play a game on conventions that are about sensitive topics, we always introduce the X card. Whenever someone feels unease of what occurs in the scene, that person can take the X card to end the scene. No questions asked, and another scene is framed instead. I have never seen the X card been used in play, but I think that's because it brings comfort having it. The player know that the scene can always end at any moment, and is therefor in control over the situation.

      And control is what "no" is about. [edit] Control brings comfort.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nAsIf \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJune 2014 \n edited June 2014
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Yes but. :-) I think control is usually what "no" is about, but there are other reasons besides control qua control. Some people will say "no..." (implied ellipsis) if they feel something suggested was technically accurate but aesthetically wrong or illogical for a given character or location (in other words, saying "no" in this case is more like defending a mood/theme/stylistic choice, editing for continuity, or pointing out a narrative gaffe; If the gaffe can be corrected without destroying the whole suggestion, then someone usually proposes an alternate pretty quickly. That suggestion often comes from a player who isn't involved directly in the scene.) In other words, sometimes "no" means "not quite exactly", which is effectively similar to a "yes but".

      This is a picayune point in most cases, but when the call precedes the fiction it can be an important distinction.

      \n\nJune 2014 \n edited June 2014
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      In other words, sometimes "no" means "not quite exactly", which is effectively similar to a "yes but".
      \nYeah, I forgot to say that in the post, but a "Yes, but" is the same thing to me as a somewhat good way of using "No". If knowing my stand on "Yes, but" as a "No" then the sentence "I've noticed how 'Yes, but' can bring comfort..." is probably clearer.

      \n\nRickard \n\n\n

      \n\nJune 2014 \n edited July 2014
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n SPONTANEITY, part two
      This time, it will be about accepting, offering, and blocking.

      p. 105Blocking is a form of aggression. I say that because if I let two students act out a scene, where they have to say "I love you" to each other, they always almost accept each others ideas.
      It's important to help the group to be in the right state of mind. If the players are competitive, or think that it's the game master versus the players, blocking will occur as a natural response to all kinds of interaction.

      p. 108-109I call everything that an actor does for "offering". An offer can either be accepted or blocked. /.../ Blocking is anything that hinders the story to develop, or rendering the possibilities useless that the partner has earlier established. If it contribute to the story, then it's not blocking.

      "Are your name Smith?"
      "And what if it were, you disgusting creep!"

      This is not blocking, even if the answer is hostile. Another example:

      "I had enough of your incompetence, Perkins! Please leave!"
      "No, Sir"

      /.../ The other one, who talks, has accepted that he is a servant, and he has accepted the situation,

      I talked earlier about the word "No" but what's important is what the word usually leads to: blocking. This is what I mean when I said that "Yes, but" is a good version of "No". I will restrict what the other person is saying but it will hopefully lead to something else. In the same matter, when I used "Yes/No...and/but" systems, I never used "No...and/but". I preferred to instead use "No, because" so I could provide more information to create a new situation that could be solved. That's not blocking by using "No", that's offering a new situation.

      p. 112These "offer-blocking-accepting" exercises are useful outside the acting career. People who live a dull and boring life thinks their lives has become like that by chance. In reality, all people choose, more or less, what kind of events that they will experience, through their conscious patterns of blocking and submission. One student objected to this idea and said "But you don't choose your life. Sometimes, you're in the hands of people who demands and controls." I said: "Are you avoiding those people?" "Oh", he said. "I understand what you mean."
      A word of wisdom, according to me. I noticed this as well. People that had bad things happening in their lives will continue to have bad things happening to them, but that's because they don't dare to choose paths other than wrong ones. I've seen my fair share of people who finally got into a good relationship or any other kind of healthy life, but screwed it up because they didn't think that they deserved it.

      (I do wonder there is a mistranslation in "conscious" from English to Swedish because I would say it's an unconscious pattern. It's possible that it's a \xbbconsciousness\xab that Johnstone is talking about, because that's the word that is used everywhere else. "...through their patterns of the consciousness..." That word assumes both the conscious and unconscious mind.)

      p. 115"Yes, but . . ."
      This is a well known "accept-and-block" exercise. /.../ I will describe it because there are two ways of doing it, that gives opposite results\u2014and it says something about the nature of spontaneity. /.../ If you do this exercise in a bad way, B will come up with the answer before he starts to talk.
      "Excuse me, is that your dog?"
      Yes, but I'm thinking of selling him."
      "Do you want to sell him to me?"
      "Yes, but he's expensive.
      "Is he healthy?"
      Yes, but you can take him to the vet and give him an exam."
      /.../
      ...the actors has probably not that fun on stage. This because it's the logical and rational part of the consciousness that is in control. If you instead answer "Yes, but..." with enthusiasm, /.../ and answers the fist anything that appears in the consciousness, the answers will be different. /.../

      "Do I know you?"
      "Yes, but I have to go."
      "You took my money!"
      "Yeah, but I've spent them all."
      "You're a bastard!"
      "Yes, but everyone knows that."
      /.../
      It's /.../ very funny to throw yourself out with "Yes, but..." and have to end the sentence on the spot.

      What he talks about is basically to have the right state of mind, but also to force yourself to answer the first thing that comes into mind. To avoid self-censoring yourself.

      ---

      The last one is a long quote, but I want to show the difference in attitude in how "Yes, but" is handled in improv compared to roleplaying games. I feel that "Yes, but" in roleplaying games are used to create an obstacle, pretty much as in the first example. The second part is accepting what has been said and giving it a spin, even though it's blocking.

      To create obstacles in roleplaying games is an attitude that I think hurts the collaborative experience in roleplaying games. It's what make the game to become the game master versus the players. We are so damn focused on using conflicts in attempt to create uncertainty, that we haven't thought of what it really does to our games. Some games thrive on having the game master as the adversary, and that's fine, but not all games need to be like that.

      I believe that ALL Forge games suffers from this, and I think it's because the roleplaying game theorists/designers have played traditional roleplaying games and brought over a lot of stuff to their designs without thinking of what it actually does. They thought that conflicts were something that needed to be included in a game to make it interesting. In The Murder of Mr Crow, there is no conflict between the participants. There is no way of saying "No". What I used instead was social contingency from the large list of uncertainties.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nRickard \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJune 2014 \n edited July 2014
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Another major difference between roleplaying games and improv is the use of "Yes, and". In improv, it's used by another participant to accept another person's contribution, and give it a spin. This is acknowledgment, which gives an (social) intrinsic reward, as I have mentioned earlier. In roleplaying games, it's the same person that does both the description of the action and provide the additional information. This is, to me, a form of violation towards the Czege principle, even though the principle is stated in a somewhat weird way: "if the same person generates the adversity and also its resolution in an RPG, the result is boring." (source)

      The important meaning in the Czege principle is that one player should not respond to it's own action, but instead let another person respond to it. That's what interaction is. Without interaction, the game becomes "boring". "Yes, and" is all about this in improv, but the use of that phrase has lost it's original purpose in roleplaying games.

      If the same person do an action and responds to it with an "and", that is responding to it's own action. This actually happen in a similar way when someone else has to respond with "No, and". Sure, that person says "No" and explaining why\u2014which is a good thing because that person is answering someone else\u2014but what then happens is that the same person, who did the explanation of why the action didn't succeed, then do an "and" and responds to their own description.

      (And why is the Czege principle phrased like it is? Well, reread the last two paragraphs in the previous post.)
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \n\nRickard \n\n\n

      \n
      \n NARRATING WITH THEATER, part 1
      The post was too long, so I had to break this chapter into smaller bits.

      p. 124I decided that the implication should be ignored. /.../ I tell the improvisers to follow the rules and see what happens and never feel singled out from what comes out of it. If you improvise in a spontaneous matter, you have to accept that your inner self will be revealed. /.../

      Alex Comfort filmed my work once, /.../ He had just explained that I worked as a therapist, that I drew the students out to areas that were normally "forbidden" and that spontaneity were about abandoning your defenses.
      Self-censoring again. This time it's not so much about wanting to fit in and having good ideas. It's instead of the fear of what others can interpret. I remember a session that I game mastered with non-gamers (with "non-gamers", I mean no experience in roleplaying games). It's a game that is all about being creative and it takes around 1,5 hours to play, including adventure writing, explaining the rules, making characters and all of that. One participant said that she had a hard time making things up, but I reassured her that it wouldn't be a problem.

      It was. The game took almost twice the time to play, because she constantly self-censored herself so whenever it were her turn to contribute, the whole game halted because she couldn't come up with anything. In retro-perspective, I thought first that I should had stopped the game and done some exercises to release spontaneity and creativity. While this may had helped, I think it was something I did in the prep phase of the game. To start off the game, the participants draw things on a map, and she drew a mountain. It looked like a dick, which I jokingly insinuated, and it got even worse when someone drew a stick figure standing on it's snow-covered peak.

      I do think that humors is great to relieve tension but in this case, I brought out her implication in the open, thus making her more anxious for what else that could had come out of her while we were playing. She then over-thought her contributions in attempt to self-censor herself. Self-censoring is an attitude that hurts creativity.

      p. 125-126When you decided to ignore the implication, it's possible to understand exactly what a narrating progression is, because you can concentrate on the structure. /.../ Even the smallest child knows that a story isn't a sequence of events.
      /.../
      The problem with such a sequence is that there isn't a point where it can end, or more rightly: it can end anywhere. You wait conscientiously on something new to begin, not on free association but on reincorporation.
      Johnstone then gives an example of a man that's being chased by a bear, and the story ends when the man is transformed into a bear and realize that he was the bear that chased him. He brings up what implications that the story probably had, which shouldn't be important, because it disrupts his ability to do free association.

      What he also brings up is the basic structure for creating a story. Free association and then reincorporate. I think it's really important to establish things; to establish things in roleplaying games to be used later. Sow and harvest. Setup and payoff. You could say that to buy a skill is to establish it, but it's not established until it comes out in play. I really like the first scene in Dogs in the Vineyard where you play out a scene based on one of the traits that your character got. It not only makes the player more invested in the trait, because framing a scene about an object is spending time on it, the scene also establish the trait for the other participants.

      I wish more roleplaying games used setup and payoffs. I wish they pointed this out, incorporated it into the structure of play, as an important part of story building.

      p. 130The improviser must be a human who moves backwards. He sees where he has been, but he never worries about what to come. His story can bring him anyway, but he still needs to "balance" it and give it's form by remembering episodes left "hanging" and reincorporate them.
      You can see this A LOT in stand-up and any form of improvisational comedy. For the latter, it's not even like the comedians think about what they are doing (or how), it's just the way they are. It comes naturally for them. We, roleplaying gamers, should to do this too.

      p. 143-144Suppose that I say "Imagine a box". A student can anticipate that I should follow up with something like "What's inside?" Instead I say "Who has put you there?".
      /.../
      We all laugh. I presume that it's because of the implied homosexuality. If I singled that out, then the student would feel like he had to put up his guard. I ignore the implication but concentrating myself instead on pulling the answers from the student as fast as possible. /.../ To answer these questions are easy. The hard part is to ask them, because you need a new angle for each question.
      I did some hard cutting in the quotes, but I wanted to repeat the message from previous quotes rather than having a coherent text.

      I always say "Ask leading questions instead of suggesting" to a group that I'm about to game master when I talk about helping each other out in a collaborative matter. Why? Because leading questions makes the person take a stand. If I ask "Is it raining?", that would be a very leading question, because it's obvious that I want it to rain. To leave it up to the other person gives that person a choice but also control (the power of be able to use "no"), compared to if I said "It is raining".

      But what Johnstone writes in the last sentence is brilliant and something that I will take with me into future session. You shouldn't only ask leading questions, but also bring new angles to every new question.
      \n
      \n

      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n NARRATING WITH THEATER, part 2

      p. 148One-word-at-the-time-letters usually goes through four stages: (1) The letters are usually careful and filled with nonsense and hidden sexual implications. (2) The letters are obscene and psychological. (3) They are filled of religious feelings. (4) Finally they express vulnerability and loneliness.

      Improvisations goes through the same stages if you don't censor them and work in with the same group over time.
      If I apply this to some of my own experiences. (1) Dick mountain! (2) Surreal events in order to wait for something to happen. (3) As me being an atheist in a secular country, I'm having a hard time to imagine any of this taking place, but the religious feeling is probably replaced with something else. Perhaps a sense of meaning (WHAT)? (4) Immersion\u2014being invested in something, like a story, the setting, or a character's emotions to mention a few different things.

      p. 154An improviser can study status transactions and how you develop an improvisation and "reincorporation" and he can learn how to associate freely and how to spontaneously develop narration through the form of the theater, and still feel it hard to "compose" a story. But this has actually to do with aesthetic and conceptual reasons. He shouldn't spend energy to invent stories, but to breaking "routines".

      If I say "make up a story", most people will get paralyzed. If I say describe a "routine" (a progress, that constantly goes in one direction, after expected templates), there will be no problem.
      /.../
      Most people tries to create more interesting routines, which doesn't solve the problem [with "composing a story", my remark]. It can be interesting to have a veterinarian give an elephant a rectal exam, or show a brain surgery performing a delicate operation, but these activities remains routines. If two caretakers breaks the routine by performing a brain surgery, or a winder cleaner starts to exam the elephant, then it's likely that this would generate a narrating progression.
      I talked about this before, that breaking routines creates a change and changes are always interesting (give or take). This is why, for example, we got experience rules in roleplaying games, even if they focus more on change in one direction (being better). Breaking routines can also create an uncertainty, to create a curiosity of what to come, which is one of the HOWs that makes us engaged in an activity.

      Note that asking questions from different angles is a form of breaking routines. \xbbA student can anticipate that I should follow up with something like "What's inside?" Instead I say "Who has put you there?".\xab

      p. 156For a lot of the students, the imagination ends at the moment when they realize that the routine they are describing is about to be fulfilled. They realize that the routine needs to be interrupted, otherwise they wont feel so unimaginative. Their problem is that they are not aware of what's gone wrong. They they finally understood the purpose of "breaking routines", they wont get stuck due to lack of imagination.
      I tried the idea of breaking routines during this last year, and it's strange how your mind can take twists and turns in a way you can't even imagine, which sparks new ideas from your own improvised ideas. I do think that you can't think things through, when using this technique, but have instead to be spontaneous. \xbbIt's /.../ very funny to throw yourself out with "Yes, but..." and have to end the sentence on the spot."\xab Please note the mental state instead of the technique. "Throw yourself out" is more important than how you actually being spontaneous.

      ---

      This was the final chapter of the four I talked about in the first post in this "series". There is one more about masks, but I don't really see the relevance. Perhaps I will come back to that later, if I take up on Jung's personalities (masks) or reading about trance. Johnstone talks about trance and hypnosis, and some of what he write is highly connected to flow (theory).

      \nAsIf sent me a link to Fun in a Different Way.\n
      Gary Fine took up this work in his 1983 ethnography of role-playing games, Shared Fantasy, where he described three important frames common to most or all gaming sessions2. First is the social frame, which participants inhabit as friends and fellow hobbyists. Laminated over this is the game frame, where players use rules to manipulate the fiction of the game. This fiction forms the third frame, which participants inhabit as characters. This is a useful framework that helps us understand the management of in-character vs. out-of-character knowledge, for example.
      \nThese frames corresponds to my thought about my WHATs. Group (social frame), structures (game frame), and fiction (fiction frame). I also got setting and meaning.

      The while essay talks about the other frames as non-immersive experiences, but here I am of a different opinion. You can immerse in, for example, game mechanics just as much as you can immerse in fiction. What's interesting is that the essay talks about immersion in fiction where "which participants inhabit as character", and therefor can call it calling it immersion (in character). I'm not even have characters in my WHATs because it's assumed that roles are used within the game.

      Ron Edwards once wrote:\n
      I have a lot of private emails people send me about the way that I "dismiss" immersion in my essay, often with very angry and defensive evidence that "it exists," always describing one of the above four things. Since these things are definitely not the same, and since the emotional commitment to the given person's definition seems to be so high ... well, my take is that we're better off deconstructing the issue into independent parts that address anything substantive, and leaving the term to evaporate slowly, if possible.
      \n"...deconstructing the issue into independent parts..."

      I totally agree, which I have done and talked about before.

      \n So I'm reading this book by Richard Duke, maybe you've heard of it: "Gaming: The Future's Language" (1974). It's a little hyperbolic in its social predictions, with a wide-eyed naivete that tastes like nostalgia to a first-wave cyberartist like myself, but nonetheless still thought-provoking today. It's always good to look back at earlier research to see which stones we've left unturned in our mad dash toward "The Future's Language". Duke's central thesis is that the primary purpose of virtually all games is to convey gestalts, i.e. holistic representations of complex situations. Here is an excerpt from the preface:

      "A careful review of the variety of products currently available as serious games turned up the startling disclosure that they seem to share no single characteristic: neither subject matter nor technique, nor duration, nor client, nor audience configuration, nor paraphernalia, nor style. In one instance, the logic of math is illustrated by dice (wooden cubes) in an exercise requiring less than an hour; in another instance, a large and carefully-trained staff, supplemented by a mammoth computer, confronts an audience of 50 to 100 people with an exercise of a week's duration. In one, role-playing is emphasized, and in another, human decision-making is emphasized in an almost sterile man-machine context. A given exercise uses a board and players move physical objects about in one instance; in another, visual aides are completely absent. Still another exercise closely mimics or simulates some real-world phenomenon, while another is a completely abstract phenomenon. Curiously, professionals have no difficulty at all in alluding to all of these as games, or addressing the phenomenon they use as "gaming" even though the particulars are so varied and diffuse.

      On the assumption that several thousand professionals did, in fact, understand the phenomenon in practice even though the theory was not coherently expressed, I, deductively, set about in an effort to determine the nature of games. The process was slow, and entailed the identification of all terms or descriptors employed within the profession. At the worst moment, over one thousand such terms had been identified. As the effort proceeded to organize these terms, a unifying perspective began to emerge - games are a form of communication."

      Thought you might wanna check it out.

I'm always very interested when someone comes to the same conclusion, but from a different background. It's on my to-read list. I also got three books on my to-get-and-read list.

      Greg Costikyan - Uncertainty in Games
      Thomas M Malaby - Beyond Play: A New Approach to Games
      Mihaly Csikszentmihalyi - The Psychology of Happiness

      \n Talking about happiness, here is a video about positive psychology.



      It mentions the flow state (Czikszentmihalyi) and how it gives an intrinsic reward (instead of XP, loot and other extrinsic rewards) but what it mainly talks talks about are the five pillars.\n
      • Positive emotion.
      • Engagement
      • Relationship
      • Meaning
      • Accomplishment
      \n
      Branches to these pillars are
      \xd7 Flow State.
      \xd7 Mindfulness. I really should read about trance and yoga and even try to practice it.
      \xd7 How Learned Positivism comes with hard work. Learned Positivism consists of joy, possibility, creativity, happiness.
      \xd7 Good Work. Excellence at doing good work, and that comes with social responsibility, are autotelic.
      \nThese things leads to fulfillment, and I think that's a reason for playing games. I should dig deeper into this. What I found is so delightful is that I found a source talking about meaning, relationship and social responsibility, and how creativity, autotelic tasks and possibility/accomplishment are part of fulfillment and the feel of happiness. And that it takes work. Meaning and group are part of my WHATs. Creativity and accomplishment are part of the WHY Expression (and slightly Competition). The combination of WHATs and WHYs, connected through the HOWs (decision, uncertainty, effort [=work]), are what I think creates engagement. Engagement is what I want from games. The result of engagement is happiness.

      It may sound very hippie-like, but this is what I want to discover. Why we play games, and how we can design games do to achieve that.

      \n You've read McGonigal, right?
      Some of her work is vague gamification but still, this is a major aspect of her design approach.

      \n I watched her videos on her homepage and read through her articles where this one summarize it all. I haven't looked up all the sources yet, but I think that will take me 1-2 months worth of work. Something tells me that her videos has given a good overview so it's really low on my to-read list. I love her for her quirkiness when it comes to how she thinks about games, because it feels like a fresh breeze to me. She's also a good source for counterarguments for "computer games creates violence", "games are a waste of times" or similar statements.

      Her sister Kelly is a health psychologist. I have checked in some of her work too, because it relates to her sister's work.

      \n "Reality is Broken" is worth reading. In the book she approaches the above themes on a case-by-case basis, explaining in detail how and why this game pertains to that human need. She uses not only her own games but also games created by others as specific examples. She also gets beyond computer-based games, talking about ARGs and gamelike physical activities (which brings it closer to our realm). It's a bit chirpy, but much more approachable than most of the online essays. In fact I view the online essays as reference material supporting the book's theses.

      \n Talked to a non-roleplaying gamer friend of mine about my two page game The Murder of Mr. Crow, and when I said that you needed pen and paper to write down some of the information, he protested and said that most people who play games don't want to write things down. Instead, he said, use cards to convey the information.

      I agree. In my game, the players need to choose characters from a list and write them down. I could as easily have made cards to be drawn instead.

      We're too accustomed to using a pen for bookkeeping that we're taking that for granted. His thoughts made me look at roleplaying game design in a different light.

      \n

      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      Intrinsic motivation

      \nI talked about mechanical rewards before and I'm a strong believer in the overjustification effect and Cognitive Evaluation Theory. These two theories, based on observations, shows that tangible rewards (ex. money, prices, XP) for non-physical work don't have any influence on, or even can decrease, the intrinsic motivation (the "feel good" of doing a task). Social reward or tangible rewards for competition are the only ones that can increase the intrinsic motivation. Here is another source for that kind of thinking.


      Even though I cut to about half the way into of the video (36m20s), the seminar as a whole is worth watching (and also their Beyond Dungeons and Dragons).

      And while I'm on the subject of intrinsic motivation. I recently read Consider It Done! Plan Making Can Eliminate the Cognitive Effects of Unfulfilled Goals by E. J. Masicampo and Roy F. Baumeister, and I came to think of a story told by the great Swedish athlete Tony Rickardsson. He was into speedracing and when he won his fist world championship, it took him years to win a second time. Why? Because he started to focus on winning. It wasn't until he went back and started to enjoy his own sport that he won the championship again, and he did that five years in a row. Here is a quote from the scientific paper (page 15):\n
      The present work, in contrast, examined process-based rather than outcome-based thinking. Participants reflected on and committed to a specific plan for attaining their goals, which has been shown to greatly increase rather than decrease goal achievement

      /.../

      Our work therefore revealed how planning for the future can positively impact goal management. Zhang et al. (2007) showed, quite importantly, that a focus on future successes can be costly.
      \nWhat they basically say is that if you focus on the success, the intrinsic motivation will decrease. If you instead focus on the process of achieving your goal, the motivation will increase. Based on the story from Tony Rickardsson and several other athletes, I think the opposite is true too. Think of the fun, and success will follow.

      I don't really know how this revelation can benefit our hobby but I'm sure it will come up in the future.

      \n I've seen many GMs posting about how they're literally intimidated by the rules of certain games, afraid of doing it wrong, basically. This can cause them to procrastinate for months (or sometimes forever) before actually running a game, or running it a second time. This may be the obverse of focusing on success - we can call it "focusing on failing" - but it has equally devastating effects.

      People talk a lot about "flow" but they're almost always looking at it from a player's perspective. I am equally interested in the GM's flow, because in a trad game, that's an aspect of play that affects everything else.

      Autotelic Tasks and Operant Conditioning

      \nDuring the 1930s, a man called B. F. Skinner created a box, an operant conditioning chamber, with a button for pigeons to peck on. He showed how he could affect their button pressing through a tangible reward mechanism (food). What he later discovered was that humans were affected in the same way as pigeons, and that he could control their active choice through the reward. Extra Credits talks more about this:



      Chris Bateman has a summary of Skinner's results over at one of his blogs:\n
      Fixed Ratio Schedules: these provide rewards after a fixed number of actions. They produce a high level of activity and are easy to understand, but after the reward is achieved, there is a pause. XP in CRPGs is an example - although the gearing of XP systems is exponential, the intent is that the player is constantly moving up to tougher foes, thus keeping a constant ratio of kills to level. /.../

      Variable Ratio Schedules: these provide rewards after a random number of actions - like a slot machine. You keep putting in coins, because at some point it will pay out. These also produce a high rate of activity and interest, but they tend to block exploration - as the player will stick with the reward schedule until it is exhausted, or until they burn out on it. Effective with all play styles - but burnout is always a risk. /.../

      Fixed Interval Schedule: that is, a reward is provided after a set amount of time. This provides better control over the rate of reward, and comes with the same post-reward pause as a fixed ratio schedule. Indeed, pauses are inherent to fixed schedules of all kinds. An example is the new items in the shop each day in Animal Crossing - the player comes back on future days to see what's new.

      Variable Interval Schedule: like the variable ratio schedule, this produces a steady rate of activity with no pauses - but its not as intense as the variable ratio schedule, because players quickly learn that their actions are independent of the reward. Good for encouraging a player to come back to certain places in a game, however, if a reward appears in certain places 'at random'.
      \nSome players say that they enjoy rolling dice [to see if they succeed]. Why? Because it follows a Variable Ratio Schedule, and strikes the same response in the brain as playing slot machines. I'm personally not keen on using exploits in human psychology to make my games more attractive, and an ethical discussion should be held around this. Of course, dice rolls also brings uncertainty but sometimes at the cost of the decision-making. If you increased your chances to a 90 % chance of succeeding, that last 10 % will tell you that all that planning you did was useless. That said, dice can be used as a "fun" element to bring uncertainty and therefor curiosity of what to come. I see no harm in random encounter tables, as an example.

      A Different View on Rewards
      Chris Bateman also lists several different kinds of rewards on that page: Currency Rewards, Rank Rewards, Mechanical Rewards, Narrative rewards, Emotional Rewards, New Toys, New Places, Completeness, Victory. Click on the link above to read more about them.

      I think all these are examples of WHY someone wants to play: Competition, Expression, Sensation, Exploration, and Destruction. I believe that a task should be autotelic, meaning that doing the task has a purpose of it's own. This is a "No shit, Sherlock" moment but to be able to create a game that is "fun", you need to attract the right target audience that thinks that the task in the game is fun to do on it's own. You can't play Ludo, expecting Chess. You can't play a railroad, expecting collaborative storytelling. Seek out what you think is fun, or take in a different approach of fun, and enjoy that game for what it is. That is why it's important to make the reader understand your game's WHY.

      Even Mih\xe1ly Cs\xedkszentmih\xe1lyi said that, to be able to reach flow state, the practitioner need to want to do the task in the first place. Wikipedia: "... identify the following six factors as encompassing an experience of flow /.../ 6. experience of the activity as intrinsically rewarding, also referred to as autotelic experience".

      I've seen many GMs posting about how they're literally intimidated by the rules of certain games, afraid of doing it wrong, basically. This can cause them to procrastinate for months (or sometimes forever) before actually running a game, or running it a second time. This may be the obverse of focusing on success - we can call it "focusing on failing" - but it has equally devastating effects.
      \nGood point. A topic that to needs to be noticed more.\n
      People talk a lot about "flow" but they're almost always looking at it from a player's perspective. I am equally interested in the GM's flow, because in a trad game, that's an aspect of play that affects everything else.
      \nI wonder if we ever talk about the flow state even about the players. Or we do, but we call it "immersion" while computer game theorists use "presence", but I think it's more about creating group flow, because what the game master does is constantly exchanging ideas with the players. Group flow hasn't been studied enough to say for sure how it's achieved but I know that I experienced it when I played hockey and soccer.

      Come to think of it, I wonder if I haven't experienced flow as a game master when playing a fish tank, because I can recall times when it felt like the scenario played itself. I know writers say the same thing when they write, how "the characters seem to live a life of their own", and that is often used as an example of one type of flow state.

      \n So... If we look at the structure of Inspectres, there's this mechanic which is waiting for a certain number of "franchise dice" (successes) to occur before the mission can end. It's not an arrangement of pre-existing elements (the scenes are all impromptu), and it doesn't have a predetermined fictional end state, but instead it has a metafictional end-state (number of franchise dice).

      Is this ballistic play, or something else?

      \n\nRickard \n\n\n

      \n\nSeptember 2014 \n edited September 2014
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I look at it as a pacing mechanism, just like playing to earn a certain amount of victory points in board games. You can shift it up or down to create shorter and longer games. It affects the length of the story, but not the content of the story.

      [edit] The term \xbbballistic play\xab is born from other discussions. Here is a summary:

      Sequential play: creates a linear structure of scenes, and can be broken down into smaller categories. \xbbLinear play\xab has fixed scenes from start to end. \xbbAssembly play\xab has scenes that must happen but can happen in any order. \xbbBallistic play\xab has a fixed beginning and end but the path between those two is unclear. Sequential play is used to create a Sensation for the players.\n
      - - - - -
      1 2 3 4 5 = linear play

      - - - - -
      3 5 2 1 4 = assembly play

          - - - - -
         /  \\ / \\ / \\
      - - - - - - - - -
      1  \\  / \\ / \\ / 5 = ballistic play
          - - - - -
      \nThe minuses are scenes, and the numbers shows in what order they appear in.

      Flow, Leadership and Joy in Work

      by Mih\xe1ly Cs\xedkszentmih\xe1lyi

      This man started a journey to find happiness. He found flow.

      This will be a translation back to English from the Swedish translation. I talked about flow earlier, as a state of happiness. All these quotes are based upon heavily grounded research and aren't just philosophical ideas. If anyone would like it, I could give the resources that his statements are based upon. As for now, to avoid wall of text, I wont.

      I will write my thoughts during the reading of the book, and how what I read can be applied to roleplaying games.

      THE TASK TO CREATE FLOW\n
      Not that materialism is bad in itself, because up to a certain level, we get happier of owning and using our possessions. All humans are in a way materialists. /.../ To be dependent of objects to reach happiness leads to an escalation of a zero-sum game: just as addiction demands constant doses of new acquisitions, and if someone doesn't have more gadgets than its neighbor, they don't really have anything of great value.
      p. 46
      \nI think this is important to realize when it comes to rewards in roleplaying games.\n
      To reach your potentials, which usually creates happiness, depends of two simultaneous processes. /.../ The first one is differentiations where one realize that we are unique individuals, responsible for our own survival and satisfaction, willing to develop our unique self wherever it may take us, meanwhile we enjoy how we express ourselves in action. The second process is about integration or the insight that no matter how unique we are, we're also totally encapsulated in a network of relations with other people, with cultural symbols and objects, and the surrounding environment.p. 47
      \nIn Feng Shui, you got -2 if you described actions in a repetitive way. I never played with that rule because it would stand in the way of contribution - the feeling of being heard by expressing yourself. Someone should be able to contribute without having to be judged by someone else, and to get feedback in form of being listened to so you feel that you done something both for and with the group.

      In Feng Shui 2, Robin D Laws removed that rule.

      Do note that getting a -2 for doing a boring description is the same thing as getting +2 for doing a good one. It's just different sides of the same coin. Remember what I said about rewards in roleplaying games. What do they really do psychologically?

      If you want people to do something, make them do it because they think it's fun. That's self-expression/differentiation. Create structures in form of rules or techniques to make them realize what they are supposed to do. Don't bribe them with rewards; that's only designing to give them a false choice and to use an exploit in the human mind (addictive behavior).\n
      A human that is fully differentiated and integrated becomes a complex individual - an individual that has the best chances to live a happy, vital, and meaningful life.p. 47
      \nA player that can both feel integrated in the group while having the chances to pursuit self-expression has the best chances to experience a fun and meaningful session.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nRickard \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nSeptember 2014 \n edited December 2014
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n HAPPINESS IN ACTION, part one
      I talked about the flow state before and eight components that can be present in various degrees, even to a degree where it's non-existent, when flow is reached. This chapter talks about these eight points below so I will only quote parts of it that I think are food for thoughts.\n
      1. A challenge activity that requires skills
      2. The merging of action and awareness
      3. Clear goals
      4. Direct feedback
      5. Concentration on the task at hand
      6. The sense of control
      7. The loss of self-consciousness
      8. The transformation of time
      \n
      In contrary belief, happiness is nothing that just comes to us. It's something we make happens, and it comes as a result of us doing our best.p. 57
      \nOne thing that bothers me with flow is that it seems like it focuses a lot on competence; about mastery of task. This is what Theory of Fun also is about, possibly inspired by flow. Cs\xedkszentmih\xe1lyi do talk about reading books as the most common flow state (p. 60), and watching a play can be another passive way of achieving flow. So the quote above is not really about doing an activity, it's about seeking activities.

      I will return to this in the next part.\n
      The moment where you find pleasure can be both physical and mentally painful; but because the pleasure consist of triumph over the powers of entropy and decadence, it will strengthen the spirit. Pleasure is the foundation for memories that, when you look back, enlighten life and gives confidence for the future.
      p. 58
      \nPleasure comes from looking back at what you accomplished during a roleplaying session.\n
      ...you get so involved in the task and all its complexity that you get absorbed by it. /.../ Flow is often the result to be together with others in a close collaboration.
      p. 60-61
      \nThe words in the Swedish language can be combined to create new words. Samber\xe4ttande is a Swedish roleplaying term for \xbbcollaborate storytelling\xab. It consist of the word "sam-" and "-ber\xe4ttande". The last word means narrating but the first one means both togetherness (samh\xf6righet) and cooperation (samarbete). There have been many sessions when I played samber\xe4ttande (collaborative storytelling) where I had flow, and the feeling of togetherness and cooperation were a huge part of that.\n
      The real pleasure lies within the steps you take towards the goal, and not actually achieving it. /.../ To care excessively about the end result often
      p. 63-64 (Clear goals)
      \nThis brings me back to when I talked earlier about the scientific paper Consider It Done! Plan Making Can Eliminate the Cognitive Effects of Unfulfilled Goals.\n
        "The present work, in contrast, examined process-based rather than outcome-based thinking. Participants reflected on and committed to a specific plan for attaining their goals, which has been shown to greatly increase rather than decrease goal achievement /.../ Our work therefore revealed how planning for the future can positively impact goal management. Zhang et al. (2007) showed, quite importantly, that a focus on future successes can be costly."
      \n
      Feedback can come from colleagues or foreman commenting what you do, but it's best when the activity itself gives that information.
      p. 64 (Direct feedback)
      \nI always found it hard to create a system where the participants praised each other, because it seemed kind of meta and artificial (and "fan mail" is NOT the answer). I talked a lot about making people contribute and to make them interact with each other. One part of interaction is responding, which is a feedback. I'm currently experimenting with techniques that creates a comfort within the group by building up a framework for interaction. That's when the activity creates feedback, by using the participants, rather than adding a guideline "outside the system" that the participants should cheer for each other.\n
      A good flow activity offers many levels of complexity
      p. 66 (The merging of action and awareness)
      \nFocus on the tactical aspect one day and the creative one the next. I will tell you why in the next part.\n
      You don't need to force the mind to think sharply, as when you try to solve a mathematical problem; on the contrary, the process seems effortless /.../ When \xbbthinking\xab begins the melting between the author and writing starts to break. The poet tried to form ideas in words on a conscious way instead of letting the words flow and fall in place naturally. But when a poet is in flow, he concentrates instead on the words that appears in the mind, he cherish them and fight to understand their meaning - rather than forcing them to follow his own will.
      p. 70 (The merging of action and awareness)
      \nWhen I talk about the system of roleplaying games and how they should cherish interaction\u2014of how they should be looked upon as a conversation\u2014this is what I mean. It should be a constant back and forth where one participant takes the other participants' actions into mind. This is what happens during a fruitful session of collaborative storytelling, but IMHO also during a session of a traditional roleplaying game, where the game master cherish the players' ideas instead of forcing them to follow an already assigned route (sequential play).

      I know situations where the scenario seemed to play itself. When no matter what the players came up with, I had a proper response. Where I felt I was in full control, even when the players surprised me. Things happened. Things I couldn't foresee. Time and space disappeared. It was just us; we and the activity we're doing. All together in one system.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nRickard \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nSeptember 2014 \n edited December 2014
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n HAPPINESS IN ACTION, part two
      The book tends to use general words like "pleasure", "ecstasy" and "happiness". Words that can mean anything to anyone. I'm more curious of HOW or WHY to reach those than what these words actually mean to each and every one.\n
      Your mind can't handle too many stimuli at the same time. If we really concentrate our attention on one particular task - independent if it's a question about mountain climbing or compose music - we can't perceive anything outside this narrow field of attention.
      p. 72 (The merging of action and awareness)
      \nMixing different playstyles, different WHYs combined with WHATs, breaks flow. I never liked how Mouse Guard mixed both tactical play with narration with dilemma. I never felt it had a nice flow with that game as when I played games that focused surely on tactical play, narration (in form of descriptive challenges), or dilemmas. Focus on the tactical aspect one day and the creative one the next.\n
      When you're in flow, the activity demands full attention, and the problems that bothers you in the everyday life have no chance to make any presence in the mind. This is the reason a pleasurable experience creates a sense of ecstasy, the feeling of be in another world. /.../ The world of flow is not only limited in the space but also time: because attention must focus on the now, events that happened in the past or future wont fit in the mind.
      p. 57 (The transformation of time)
      \n"Don't plan ahead" is a mantra used in improv. The meaning of that mantra is that if you plan ahead, you will be more inclined to stick to that and block any attempts that might change that outcome. You will be less inclined to take other contributions into consideration. The quote above is another reason why you shouldn't plan ahead. You could, and should, have a clear goal of where you want to go, but not assume the outcome of it.

      Also, don't focus on the outcome of success (the result of the plan). That's something that is in the future. Focus on what happens now; the journey to the future.\n
      But what they forget is their social persona /.../ with all the responsibility that are tied to that. It's an ecstatic feeling to, in a blink of an eye, be relieved from the consciousness about yourself, about ambitions and defeats, fears and wishes. /.../ It's common to forget who you are during a flow experience, but afterwards your self-awareness returns in a stronger form than before.
      p. 80-81 (The loss of self-consciousness)


      \nWhat he talks about is the reason why people seek different kinds of immersion. I see an explanation of why people seek character immersion - bleed in LARP - where the character's emotions bleed into your own mind. This can happen for a chess player as well, where the world is the chess board. Presence is another form of immersion in computer games, where the game world becomes the real world for the player. I've felt emotions for characters in a book or a movie, and I have lost myself in stories told in a collaborative manner with my friends around a table.

      Listen to what Heather Anne Campbell's reason is to why she does improv. It's like she's talking about roleplaying games. (at 4 min 24 sec)

      \n
      It's a mistake to draw the conclusion that flow only occurs where there isn't anything at stake, when you performing an activity that you have chosen yourself, that is exciting and has no consequences for the real life.
      p. 83
      \nTo connect to what he first wrote in this this chapter. Flow is something you seek out. Not seeking out, as in doing certain activities that brings flow, but something you can seek out in any activity. Try to find the purpose of your activity. Make it autotelic (doing a task for the purpose of doing the task). That is what is intrinsically rewarding. You can be a well-paid surgeon but if you love what you do, no money will make it either more or less fun. Compare the surgeon's salary to mechanical rewards in roleplaying games. Compare this to how roleplaying games seldom conveys why anyone would play that game. Roleplaying games should be better at guiding the participants in finding what's fun with that particular game. In finding the purpose of playing that game.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nRickard \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nSeptember 2014 \n edited December 2014
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n FLOW AND DEVELOPMENT
      I didn't think I would benefit at all from reading this chapter.\n
      ... one of the main requirements for flow, the balance between challenge and skill.
      p. 88
      \nThis is the main reason why I think flow is mainly about skill mastery. The book said on page 60 that reading a book is the most common source of flow but in this chapter, Cs\xedkszentmih\xe1lyi talks purely about challenge. This chapter can mostly be summed up by the following picture.

      \n
      Flow attracts progression. If you want to stay in flow, you must do progress, and learn more to ascend to a higher level of complexity /.../ Anyone would quickly get tired on any kind of labor if the challenges remains on the same level.
      p. 89
      \nI'm going to take this out of its context, where Cs\xedkszentmih\xe1lyi talks about increasing in skill. I have previously talked about how change keeps up the interest. I do agree that progress, often in form of increasing skills, leads to change but it's not the only way change can occur.

      Jim Butcher talks about the aftereffects of conflict in stories, and how they really are the interesting part because they are the consequences of the conflict. The conflict brings uncertainty but it's the consequences that are the changes.\n
      Everything from textiles to weapons, from houses to meals becomes more complex: harder to manufacture but easier to use. /.../ The evolution of culture is based on the differentiation and integration of earlier manufactured objects and previous knowledge. /.../ Groups of people can also be described as more or less complex
      p. 95
      \nRemember from the earlier chapter that a complex person is a person who is fully differentiated (self-expressing) and integrated (with others). The first cameras was really simple but hard to use. The more they developed, the more differentiated they became but integrated with the users, the more complex they became but also simpler to use.

      The same thing happens with a group. Inside jokes are born, social "house rules" are created, hierarchies are set, and playstyles are formed. The participants becomes more and more integrated with each other and the group becomes more and more differentiated. Shortcuts can be made; shortcuts that will make it harder for someone new to arrive to the group; shortcuts that we wont realize that we created ourselves.

      This ... is ... why I made this thread. Our hobby is still young but we have learned a lot over time. We got artifacts from other games and previous knowledge from game creators, but who knows how a camera works nowadays? We know that certain things work but not why. It's too complex for us to realize how the group is created, even if it's easy to use. I want to draw knowledge from that, to explain the first parts and how they fit together and what result they bring. I want to rediscover what we take for granted, mostly so I can continue to differentiate them and then integrate with others. I would like roleplaying games to build the group and help out differentiate and integrate it. Because I feel that we have come as far as we can. Something new must happen, and something groundbreaking.

      I'm not the only one who thinks this, because the whole OSR movement is basically doing the same thing. Their WHY is however not the same as mine so I have to find my own way.

      ---

      I thought I wouldn't benefit at all from reading this chapter. I was wrong.

      The next part of the book is about flow and organizations and the reason why I picked up this particular book about flow in the first place.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nRickard \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nSeptember 2014 \n edited December 2014
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n WHY FLOW WONT APPEAR AT WORK
      The chapter dives deeper into some these points.\n
      1. A challenge activity that requires skills
      2. The merging of action and awareness
      3. Clear goals
      4. Direct feedback
      5. Concentration on the task at hand
      6. The sense of control
      7. The loss of self-consciousness
      8. The transformation of time
      \n
      The goal for the company management is to create value with help of the work of the people who are working together on a common task. /.../ The challenge for anyone who wants to create an environment that attracts and keeps enthusiastic and enterprising coworkers is to understand why people WANT to work in the first place, and to create the conditions that meets these needs.
      p. 115-116
      \n"Value" is often economic growth in companies, but what is the value in roleplaying games? WHY would anyone want to play roleplaying games?\n
      ...a company management has /.../ three options. The first is to make the objective conditions on the workplace as appealing as possible. The second is to find different ways to fill the work with meaning and value. The third is to steer the working moral in the organization in a positive direction...
      p. 118
      \nIf we would start looking at roleplaying games as work, because they are demanding like all games (Bernard Suits: Games are inefficient. The rules impose obstacles that prevent the player from reaching their goal through the most efficient means), WHY would anyone want to do it?\n
      ...contemporary jobs seldom gives adequate feedback.p. 125
      \nNothing in most jobs says anything about that person's skill. A coworker is replaceable, as in comparison to a carpenter where every movement with the tools are sign of that person's skill.

      When I pitch roleplaying games on conventions or bigger gatherings, I focus on the purpose of the game. Why would anyone want to play that particular game? I noticed that I can't do that with boardgames because it's assumed that we play it to win. Roleplaying games are different here; more diverse, if I may say so. Where boardgames have different requirements of skills but with the same goal - to win - roleplaying games seldom have the same purpose. One game can be to have your character's emotion bleed into your own feelings, another could be to create a plot together, a third about discover how the history affects the present day. Some roleplaying games are played to Compete for victory, but a lot focuses on Expressing yourself, to Explore, to get a Sensation, or simply to Destroying structures or otherwise feeling powerful.

      These things are the feedback that the roleplaying game should give. Rewards is a subcategory to feedback, and I would like to say that all kinds of feedback is a reward if it has anything to do with the participant's purpose of doing that activity. If you want to play to loose your own persona into a character, the game should send feedback that helps you achieve that. If you want to do tactical decisions, the game should feed you more tactical choices back to you. The more you do this kind of circular exchange, the more invested you become in the activity. That's how flow/immersion/presences/being in the zone is achieved. That's how you gain intrinsic rewards. That's how you reach fulfillment.\n
      Another feature in many jobs is the lack of control. /.../ Both these complaints comes from the feeling to be reduced to a tool where any possibility to affect the company where you work.p. 127
      \nI can totally understand people that hate railroading. When I game master sequential play, I make sure that the players got control over certain areas, like how to portray their character, how they can create things in the environment, or take shortcuts in the adventure. I'm also very open with what they can and cannot change, so they don't focus on the parts where they don't have control.

      So even if they are spectators of a sensational ride, they still got control over certain areas which can affect how they will perceive the adventure. Of course, if your purpose of being a player is restricted to only control the fiction, this is not the style of play you're after.\n
      As we previously pointed out, young people grow up with different social capital in form of support from parents, education, and resources from the society. Some learn to build up a psychological capital; other don't. When they grown up enough to work, some are so brainwashed by culture that they can't see a job as anything else than work.p. 136
      \nA tiger spends energy to hunt, in hope to get more energy. Doing mental or social exercises do drain our energy - our psychological capital. Roleplaying games are work, so why don't we think of it as work? What is it we get back from it?

      It's basically what the previous chapters have talked about.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \n TO BUILD FLOW IN AN ORGANIZATION

      This will be the last chapter to comment because I didn't feel the last two that followed after this one had anything to bring. Both this chapter and the one before are mostly repetition of what has already been established.\n
      People often believe that a successful leader in the business world have to know a lot about what the organization does - namely what the product or the service that the organization has specialized on. It's certainly valuable knowledge, but the main task is to make people work effectively together.
      p. 144
      \nThe best way to improvise is to be well prepared because it will create a great feeling of control, but the game master also needs to make the group work together.\n
      You can't demand a human to do its best. You can't demand Beethoven to compose his ninth symphony. He must want to do it himself. /.../ The best way to create such organization is to provide it with the conditions needed for the employed to experience flow.
      p. 144-145
      \nThe game master must be able to create a positive group feeling (team spirit), where everyone feel comfort, creativity, willing to participate, create spontaneity, but also tickle everyone's playstyle ... or, as I prefer it, convey the playstyle so the players can adopt to it.

      What Cs\xedkszentmih\xe1lyi have in mind is to challenge the players and constantly increase the difficulty as the players' skill level increase. Not sure I agree with that because we got other ways of making the players invest more and more into the game. The previous paragraph lists the effects. I already talked about intrinsic motivation and the constant loop to create investment (see my image below).

      \n
      It's impossible to create an environment that achieves flow without the involvement of the company management. The leaders must accept the thought that products, winnings and market shares are put behind the emotional wellbeing of the employers.
      p. 151
      \nThe game master shouldn't focus on the adventure, or anything related to it, but instead be aware if everybody enjoys the adventure.\n
      It's hard for the employers to concentrate to fulfill the companies goal if they don't understand those goals - or worse, misunderstanding them /.../ There can be many reasons why goals like that aren't clear in many organizations. Firstly, the assignment for the organization /.../ isn't clear for everyone, including the highest management. /.../ Secondly, the assignment is unclear for the product management. /.../ Thirdly, it could be so that the assignment is unclear for the subordinate.p. 153, 155-157
      \nI lacked knowledge about what I wanted with my first published roleplaying game. It became a hybrid of two different playstyles. If I don't know what I want with my own game, how could I then make other people understand it? A roleplaying game should be written so that the game master can teach the players the information in it. I feel that few roleplaying games actually does that. A good game master can save any game, by imposing his or her own playstyle and making the players grok it. The players must also be open-minded with what kind of playstyles that they can accept. If someone only wants to think in a tactical way, that wont do it for a group that wants to experiment with different kinds of playstyles from now and then.\n
      To be in the loop of how well you're doing something and be well prepared on the task, it's meaningful to create routines for three sources of feedback. The first is feedback from other people. /.../ The second source of information is feedback from the work itself. /.../ The last source comes from feedback from your own personal standards.p. 164, 169-170
      \nTo be able to grow as a game master, you must be aware of what happens around the table. To take in the feedback that you are given. Are everybody enjoying what we're doing? Spolin's list of questions comes into mind that she ask herself when she's sidecoaching during a theater exercise.\n
      1. Am I giving enough energy?
      2. Am I staying overlong on mechanics?
      3. Which players need individual attention?
      4. Do they need more workshops?
      5. Are rehearsals to drawn out?
      6. Am I nagging the players?
      7. Am I attacking them?
      8. Are the actors working at odds with me?
      9. Is the problem physical or psychological?
      10. Am I just being a traffic manager?
      11. Is it necessary to stimulate more spontaneity?
      12. Am I overanxious?
      13. Am I asking them for more than they can give me at this time?
      14. Am I reaching the intuitive?
      \n

      Drama is About Change

      \nNote that the authors below are using the dramaturgic definition of "scene": a situation where something dramatic happens.

      David Mamet's Master Class Memo to the Writers of The Unit\n
      WHAT IS DRAMA? DRAMA, AGAIN, IS THE QUEST OF THE HERO TO OVERCOME THOSE THINGS WHICH PREVENT HIM FROM ACHIEVING A SPECIFIC, ACUTE GOAL. /.../ START, EVERY TIME, WITH THIS INVIOLABLE RULE: THE SCENE MUST BE DRAMATIC. IT MUST START BECAUSE THE HERO HAS A PROBLEM, AND IT MUST CULMINATE WITH THE HERO FINDING HIM OR HERSELF EITHER THWARTED OR EDUCATED THAT ANOTHER WAY EXISTS.
      \nJim Butcher About Scenes\n
      Once you've got your point of view character, the next thing you need is a GOAL for them to pursue. This needs to be an ACTIVE, SPECIFIC goal, not just something vague.
      Butcher describes this in form of a conflict, and conflict is a way to create drama. A lot of Forge games are overdoing this, like Zombie Cinema, The Shab-al-Hiri Roach, InSpectres and many (many) more, where all scenes are following the same structure: "frame a new scene, create conflict, resolve conflict, frame a new scene", et c. I found something really interesting in his following article.

      Jim Butcher About SequelsSequels are what happens as an aftermath to a scene. /.../ The heart of your character--and your reader--is in the sequel.What's interesting is that this article changes the perspective of conflicts. Where many people seem to think that conflict is the drama, but the real drama occurs in the aftermath.

      Play Passionately\n
      It is my intention to layout a few \u201cbest practices\u201d for opening yourself to the levels of creative risk that routinely produces high-impact emotional narratives. /.../ The first step is learning to define a character based on his crisis rather than his capabilities. /.../ Once we\u2019re thinking about characters in terms of the crisis and struggles they deal with,
      \nSee all these quotes as goodies. There are a lot more to find in the articles.

      Drama seems to be more about change of the character's self-image and its effect on the audience, the character itself and the surrounding world.

      "Change" is the key ingredient for good stories.
      \n
