      Plan For Change

      \nI linked to the David Mamet's article in the last post, and in that post he wrote three questions a screenwriter should ask.\n
      WHO WANTS WHAT?
      WHAT HAPPENS IF HER DON'T GET IT?
      WHY NOW?
      \nJohn Rogers got similar questions.\n
      Who wants what?
      Why can't they have it?
      Why do I give a shit?
      \nDan "Community" Harmon got the following beginning in his Story Circle.\n
      A character is in a zone of comfort,
      But they want something.
      They enter an unfamiliar situation,
      ...
      \nAll these beginnings establish drives. It doesn't matter that much what happens along the way, because the players got something to strive for. So if this is the beginning, what's the end?

      Start With the End
      Extra Credits suggests that when you start with a game's narrative, you should start at the end. I talked about change in the previous chapter and will now show examples of structures that has change in

      Joseph Campbell's The Hero's Journey end with the hero returning ... changed.

      Dan Harmon's Story Circle is his own take on the monomyth above, and it ends in the following way:\n
      7. Then return to their familiar situation,
      8. Having changed.
      \nJim Butcher about his sequels, which are the aftermaths of the conflicts.\n
      1) Allow a character to react emotionally to a scene's outcome.
      2) Allow a character to review facts and work through the logical options of his situation.
      3) They allow a character to ponder probable outcomes to various choices.
      4) They allow a character to make a CHOICE-
      \nFirst to third point is about the change. To update your perspective from the information received. When I read this, I instantly thought of the feedback loop in character immersion, the circular exchange in the skill atom and flow, and of course my own model of how the constant loop in an interaction model makes you invested.

      Do note that everything above is only one example. Other types of changes can be tilts, or the fact that you have to take the other participants contributions into account. The last is something that is typical for roleplaying games but not a lone screenwriter.re.

      Reincorporation
      The ending of a story is not purely about having things changing. With change reincorporation must happen. Otherwise no one will notice the change. Keith Johnston talks about this in Impro. You can sit and throw up new information constantly but it's not until we start to reincorporate to what has been established previously that we realize that the story is coming to its end. The Story Circle does this after half the journey. "As you might expect with a circular model like this, there is a lot of symmetry going on, and on the journey back upward, we're going to be doing a lot of referencing to the journey downward."

      This is change that creates the base for the drive. Everybody should know where everybody wants to go so people could take that into consideration. We also need to get things started, and we can do that by setting up elements from the end in the beginning so we can gain payoff by reincorporate it when it's closing towards the end. So to create "drama" we need to start in the end to establish where we want to go.

      Just as I did with this post.
      \n

      The Importance of HOW

      \nWhen I first talked about HOWs, I attached states of mind to each of the three forms of interaction. Uncertainty brought suspense, Decision tension, and with Effort came challenge. I was never satisfied with this, because it was mostly done so I could show my view of how a challenge never came with dice rolls alone. A game master could never challenge a player by throwing in a locked door. Mouse Guard has this kind of (non-)challenge in its model for adventure writing, as an example of how wide spread this misconception is. The game master in that game shall create three "challenges" that have to evoke dice rolling to be defeated.

      A question was raised during the discussion about HOW of why a fisherman continues fishing day out and day in no matter what the weather was like. Why would s/he do that?

      I also showed my view of what happened if I tied investment into the HOWs. With Uncertainty gamble would occur, with Decision you would get planning, and with Effort there would be a struggle. I'm not sure if this is true at all, but what I wanted was to show how you could create certain ways of interacting through these combination.

      Talking about change above made me think of a third thing that explains the importance of the three HOWs.
      Uncertainty is created through change. Not knowing how things will change, suspense is created.
      Decision leads to interactivity. Any tension created through decision-making enforces planning. This is however not true in the lowest form of decision-making, like deciding what kind of potato crisp that you want or if you want it at all. I will have to think more about this.
      Effort leads to participation. You want the people involved to participate in a game, no matter how easy or hard the challenge is.
      You want change, interactivity and participation but the real tools that you should use are uncertainty, decision and effort. They are the things you can grasp. Things you can use. It's like talking about fun and then showing a tool to have fun with, like skipping ropes.

      Control vs Surprise
      Greg Costikyan starts off in his book Uncertainty in Games about how we want control in our lives.\n
      Uncertainty is not, in most circumstances, a good thing. We do not wish to be uncertain about whether we can pay our bills, uncertain of the affections of the people who matter to us, uncertain about our health, or uncertain about our job prospects

      /.../

      But the reality is that we are faced with uncertainty throughout our lives\u2014and that much of our effort is devoted to managing and ameliorating that uncertainty. Is it any wonder, then, that we have taken this aspect of our lives, and transformed it culturally, made a series of elaborate constructs that subject us to uncertainty\u2014but in a fictive and nonthreatening way?

      I'm talking about games, of course.
      p. 8-9
      \nI keep thinking of how Keith Johnstone in Impro says that naysayers keep control in their lives while yaysayers are rewarded with adventure.

      Keith Burgun discusses in a seminar that a meaningful decision is somewhere in the middle of the scale between answer and guess.

      Costikyan's and Burgun's points can be narrowed down to these two scales.

      Solution <--------- x ---------> Guess
      Predictability <--------- x ---------> Uncertainty

      If there is one clear solution, no choices are to be made. Guesses are created when there aren't enough information and one choice will be as good as the other. A guess means that it will have no meaningfulness to the decision.

      Being able to predict the outcome is usually good, but not when you want to be entertained. You want some suspense of what to come. On the other hand, you should have an idea of the outcome to create expectations. Whether they are fulfilled, broken or if they come with a twist could still be left as an uncertainty.

      Looking at these two scales, the first being about Decision and the second about Uncertainty, I wonder if not all HOWs are about control vs surprise. An everyday walk is always controlled but if there wouldn't be anything different from day to day, I presume the walk would become boring. There is a balance between the inner factor, where you can control things, and outer factors like weather, sleep and other humans. (I just made up inner and outer factor. There could be two other forces instead.)

      It still doesn't say anything about the fisherman though, but I think it's more a WHY than a result of the HOW. When I talk about WHY, HOW and WHAT, I talk from a game designer's perspective and present them as tools to build an activity. I never want to use them to analyze games or participants of an activity.

      More Uncertainties

      I finished Uncertainty in Games yesterday, and Greg Costikyan presents a couple of uncertainties. Here is a summary of them, but Costikyan discusses the uses and the downfalls with each one of them.

      Performance Uncertainty: This is restricted to physical performance \u2013 reaction times, shooting things, etc. /.../

      Randomness: This is perhaps the easiest element of uncertainty to understand, and it may be the easiest to build into games. /.../

      Solver\u2019s Uncertainty: There are non-overt problems built into many games that the player must solve in order to proceed. /.../

      Player Unpredictability: What will the player do to change what was thought to be defined outcomes? /.../

      Analytic Complexity: This happens when you offer a player only a small number of choices, but all choices are difficult to choose based on available data. /.../

      Hidden Information: /.../ It can also be information you don\u2019t have yet know it exists, /.../

      Narrative Anticipation: Keeping the player uncertain how the game story will evolve. /.../

      Development Anticipation: /.../ This is the hype that precedes the release of a game, /.../

      Schedule Uncertainty: Some online games limit the amount of time or turns a player can spend in the game per day.

      Perception Uncertainty: /.../ For example, in Guitar Hero, the player must perceive musical rhythms via a flowing line of symbols and trigger the correct response at the correct time based on accurate perception of the flowing symbol line.

      Malaby\u2019s Semiotic Contingency: I already talked about this in previous post. When the meaning of the game changes, like the game Train. In that game the players are stuffing carts full of people to transfer to a location, but after half the game they discover that they are playing nazi soldiers.

      I updated this list of uncertainties with my own summaries and examples.

      However, spending time and resources on one challenge meant less time and resources for the next. There in lay the player skill and the beautiful agony.
      \nTrue, but I don't think that belongs to the pages about how to write a scenario. I need to reread the game, but it's like one paragraph in a 300+ pages book. And spending resources without knowing what to come is just a pure guess and not a meaningful decision when it comes to handling resources. The only meaning comes in the Decision if that particular situation is worth its cost to overcome, by involving the character's belief, but that's a tension through a dilemma and not a challenge. According to my categorization anyway, where Effort from the participant must be involved for a task to become a challenge. (It's however a challenge for the character.)

      This is change that creates the base for the drive. Everybody should know where everybody wants to go so people could take that into consideration. We also need to get things started, and we can do that by setting up elements from the end in the beginning so we can gain payoff by reincorporate it when it's closing towards the end.       \nYou could say that putting up stakes before a roll does exactly this, but I think it's a crude way of using that tool. In TV series, you already know what the stakes are because the conflict is about what the person cares about so you don't need to create stakes. I think a good system would create characters where each individual have a couple of goals but also personality traits. They must first be established and then either one will change during the course of the game when they are tested against each other, or if new information rises where the player (through the character) must update goals nor traits accordingly.

      \n I was down on Mouse Guard until I played it. The scenario was frustratingly linear but it worked. Yes, the challenges were dice only (though creative application of fictional elements gave more dice). However, spending time and resources on one challenge meant less time and resources for the next. There in lay the player skill and the beautiful agony.

      \n
      \n Some paragraphs I found thoughtful in Uncertainty in Games.\n
      Randomness thus has strengths: it adds drama, it breaks symmetry, it provides simulation value, and it can be used to foster strategy through statistical analysis. It has countervailing weaknesses: in excess, it imbalances games, it can foster a sense that success is a consequence of luck rather than excellent play, and it can produce frustration when a streak of bad luck affects a player. But given the ease with which it fosters uncertainty, it has a useful role to play in many games. As always, whether or not to harness randomness in a design is as much a matter of aesthetics as anything else; it has no place in a game of abstract strategy, but it is almost essential in a simulation.
      p. 86
      \nI don't fancy randomness anymore. I prefer Player Uncertainty/Social Contingency in roleplaying games.\n
      Most game design is imitative; that is, a designer typically looks to existing, successful games, adopts proven mechanics, and alters mainly theme, setting, and story (if any). Innovation tends to be incremental, the designer seeking to improve or add a new aspect to existing mechanics. In part, this is because of the conservatism of publishers, who (mostly wrongly) believe they are minimizing financial risk by minimizing design risk; in part, it is because few designers have the confidence and desire to innovate in more fundamental ways.

      Thus, most designers, most of the time, do not think about where uncertainty lies in their games; they are working within a well-known genre, and the mechanics they adopt have already been shown to produce results that please players. The uncertainties the genre creates are already tuned.
      p. 105
      \nI'm tired of randomness and how people use it to build roleplaying games in a certain way. We got terms like "GM-less" and "dice-less" because it's assumed that a roleplaying game should have those elements. If game designers started to question these fundamental things, I think we would see totally different games. They would probably do the same thing, but in a totally different matter.\n
      Typically, when designers think about tuning gameplay, they think about tuning the difficulty of existing systems. In a platformer, if the game seems too easy, you make the time window for player response narrower; if it seems too hard, you expand the time window or work to make the controls crisper and more responsive. /.../

      What designers rarely do is introduce a new and different form of uncertainty, because this is not an immediately obvious way to handle the problem. And yet, doing this can be quite powerful and lead to highly original work.
      p. 109-110
      \nRandomness are used to make things less predictable but as this post shows there are many ways of creating uncertainty. I find the notion of fine-tuning the uncertainty, to make the game harder or easier, interesting. That's something I will take with me from reading this book.

      \n Got a lot of reading material. Here is something I would like to share.

      Creating Tension Through Decision Resolution Cycles

      Here is another person, besides me, who thinks decisions creates tension.

      Also note: "cycles".
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nRickard \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2014 \n edited December 2014
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I like when game designers are controversial because that's where new ways of thinking can be found. Keith Burgun is one of these game designers and he's currently making a series of game design videos. I find personally his thoughts interesting about what is required of a game to make it a game. Each episode is only three minutes long so do check them out.



      I would like to give two of these videos extra attention.



      This one talks about what a game consist of, and it supports my thought that roleplaying games are mostly a game engine and not a game itself. His base of categorizing a task goes from toy -> simulator -> contest -> game, where each step has a requirement that has to be fulfilled to make it fit into that category. While roleplaying games have, for example, a combat system that may look like a contest, the roleplaying game itself usually lack a structure of play that involves the combat system. Instead, it's a tool for the game master to build an adventure around. It's not until the the game's structure of play says "There has to be two combats in an adventure" that the roleplaying game can be considered being at least a contest.

      ---



      The key word in this video is \xbbheuristic learning\xab (learning from doing, and what is an emotional response to learning?). The video is another source that talks about cycles in games. All my learning, that a game state is updated through constant feedback cycles, have made me wonder if I only saw half the picture when I talked about pools and procedures. When I wrote that post, I had a sequence in mind with an end result. What I learned during this last year is that I need to look at a process instead of the end result. The process comes in form of a feedback cycle.

      Realizing this turns a lot on its end. I now see game mechanics and, even more frighteningly, game designers that design game with only an end result in mind. It doesn't matter what leads up to that result or how it affects the players, it's only the result that is important. This is, to give an example, one reason why we got so much design-through-rewards in roleplaying games. If the game designer wants people to learn Korean, the only thing that matters is that they actually talk Korean. So a "good" and well accepted way of achieving this is to give the players rewards when they do that.

      Instead, I suggest creating a feedback cycle where speaking Korean is part of the whole process of playing the game. That's what the game Magicians do. Because when you design by giving tangible rewards as a carrot, that can obstruct the real purpose of why the players should do the task. Are they doing it because of the fun of doing the task or is it because if the carrot?

      Scientific research has shown that the intrinsic reward, the "feel good" feeling, of doing the task is either unaffected or decreased by extrinsic rewards (money, XP, et c.) [exceptions in first paragraph]. Games like Primetime Adventures and Burning Wheel are built around rewards because the game designer have only looked at the end result. Having the players praise the other players by giving them fan mail in Primetime Adventures can do one of two things: either the player getting the reward realize what the reward actually is about (getting praise) which means that the reward itself doesn't do anything, or the player does the task because of the reward. Either way, this is sloppy and also hurtful design born from a sequential thinking where only the end result is important to achieve, instead of looking at the whole process and what kind of result that process produces.

      Sure, both games achieve the result they intended but at what cost?
      \n
      \n
      \n

      The Major Dramatic Curve

      \n Gustav Freytag sat one day down and started to analyze Greek and Shakespearian drama. The fruit of his work was Freytag's triangle which became the Major Dramatic Curve. This five act structure is not something you can find in anything, but what's interesting is that it contains something that I feel is built into our human psyche.


      (Source: wikipedia)
      Exposition: introduces background and the character.
      Rising action: conflicting events that builds up to the climax.
      Climax: the turning point in the story and going towards tragedy or triumph for the character.
      Falling action: the constant battle is going towards its end.
      D\xe9nouement, resolution, revelation or catastroph: a conclusion of the whole story. What did the audience learn?

      It may seem like this is a formula for a whole story but this curve is constantly repeated all through the process. A more accurate representation of the dramatic curve can be seen here below:



      What this shows a multiple of the triangle going into each other, in a bigger triangle. There is a constant escalation going. This is important for what my next post is going to be about.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      Flow and the Constant Cycle of Emotions

      \nWhat I don't like about flow is that it's about the "player" getting in flow through constantly being better and better at the task, through learning from feedback, while facing harder and harder difficulty. Flow will be achieved if the skill level and the difficulty constantly match during a period of time (as shown in the second graph).



      What this boils down to is purely skill mastery but Cs\xedkszentmih\xe1lyi said that reading books is the most common source of achieving flow state (p. 60, Flow, Leadership and Joy in Work). Reading a book is not about skill mastery at all.

      Then I stumbled upon an article by Rizky Winanda. I seriously recommend his blog that contains a lot of insightful perspectives. The analyze of the Campbell's Monomyth and how the archetypes are reflections of the hero in his journey is really food for thought. The following below is taken from the first linked article.\n
      One thing that can be done is by seeing what happened inside player mind when they confront a challenge. Through my little research, I found there is a pattern that always appears when people are facing a challenge. In short, there are four important components that create the pattern: expectation, doubt, reality, and relief. Expectation will lead the player to do specific curiosity, where they only use one solution that they thing as the right. However, when they are confronted with a doubt, they will see the reality and do novelty seeking, looking all available solutions. /.../
      \n\n
      From the graph, we can see there are two types of trend. First, an upward trend that starts from relief and ends with doubt. This trend is composed of expectation that leads the player to do specific curiosity. The other is the downward trend, which starts with doubt and ends with relief. In this trend, player will do novelty seeking by looking at the reality. As stated previously, in upward trend, player only focus on one solution because player believes the solution is the answer to what s/he confronts. It means in this trend player\u2019s skill is higher than the challenge that the game provides. The opposite happens in the downward trend. In this state, player looks for all available solutions because the previous solution is not good enough. It means the challenge is greater than player\u2019s skill.

      So, I believe this pattern is what has happened in every point in flow theory. By continuously interchanging between two situations that are depicted in those two trends, it will help the player to become better (by giving a greater challenge than player\u2019s current skill). Also, it provides feeling of achievement and empowerment (by giving a challenge that player can finish by player\u2019s current skill).
      \nNow tell me that this graph about emotions in a constantly escalating curve doesn't look exactly like the dramaturgic curve. When I read "relief", "expectations" and "doubt", I think of the beginning of Dan Harmon's simplified version of the monomyth: The Story Circle.\n
      A character is in a zone of comfort, [relief]
      But they want something. [expectation]
      They enter an unfamiliar situation, [doubt]
      ...
      \nRizky Winanda continues with applying this graph to the flow graph ...... and flow finally makes sense to me. It's a constant cycle based on emotions. It's something built into our human psyche. The escalation is basically an updated (game) state, which also brings uncertainty (LeBlanc) to the activity. If we constantly escalate, then we can still take what happens in the past into account but it wont be of as much importance compared to what lies in the future.
      \n

      The Heuristic Spiral

      \n
      define: heuristic (Google)

      \u02cchj\u028a(\u0259)\u02c8r\u026ast\u026ak/

      adjective
      1.
      enabling a person to discover or learn something for themselves.
      "a \u2018hands-on\u2019 or interactive heuristic approach to learning"
      /.../
      noun 1.
      a heuristic process or method.
      \nI've been talking for six months about how gameplay are based around feedback loops, and how interaction is nothing more than responses between at least two game elements (participants, game mechanics). What I left out, mostly to make it comprehensible, is the constant escalation that occurs. The feedback from the exchange will add another layer for the next time the player enters the same procedure. So when I was talking about feedback loops, I merely talked about one specific happening in a process.

      As we can tell from the graph above of how flow is reached, we can see that the player learns more and more over time. So the feedback loop is not circular at all\u2014it's an up going spiral.


      This is an image from when I talked about how immersion is reached.

      I'm not the first to realize this. The feedback loop was theorized by Martin Heidegger back in 1927 in what he called the hermeneutic circle. Hermeneutic means basically \xbbconcerning interpretation\xab. This was then taken further by other people, but it wasn't until Bernard Perron did the same thing I did\u2014to take different perspectives and put them together\u2014that the circle became a spiral. Below is his representation of the feedback loop; the one specific procedure in a whole process. At the moment, the only important part for this post is how the interaction occurs with the player making input and then processes the output.


      (source)

      This circular exchange then included the constant escalation, due to more and more information, and it became the heuristic spiral. What's interesting is that he talks about three types of spirals:
      \xd7 Heuristic spiral of gameplay. Understanding the result of the gameplay.
      \xd7 Heuristic spiral of narrative. Understanding the fiction that most games have.
      \xd7 Hermeneutic spiral. Understanding the underlying meaning of the game.

      What's interesting about this is that it correlates somewhat to three of my WHATs: Structures, Fiction, and Meaning. The two missing are Group and Setting. So either I found two more spirals by taking in other kinds of sources than him, or two or my categories are pointless.

      There is more to talk about this, but I will leave it for later. The information I got is taken from The Video Game Theory Reader 2, In the Frame of the Magic Cycle (Chapter 6, p. 109-132) by Dominic Arsenault and Bernard Perron.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\ndavidepignedoli \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2014 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I got in touch with @Rickard and we were chatting about this topic - and he asked me to post this publicly.
      We mentioned chess - not an RPG, but a game that really fits a lot of these descriptions - it's almost as 'free' as an RPG (as in a higher number of variants than a boardgame for example) - but it has an incredible learning curve, an engaging system...

      The reason I mentioned chess is because it has some interesting concepts that can be - I believe - analyzed also in the prospective of an RPG...
      Here's the idea: of course there is the emergent complexity of the game of chess, but there are a few additional interesting concepts in a chess game, that I'd love to find a way to express in RPG game design, so that the RPG game itself would feel somehow similar to chess.

      For example - the rules are apparently simple, but as we said, with a tremendous amount of potential complexity because of the "situations" (using an RPG term) that will arise (namely, the various game openings, the development of the pieces on the board, and the knowledge of the players, etc., that make each game unique).

      Another example: all pieces start as "protected": all the pawns are protected by major pieces, and all major pieces are behind pawns, so that nothing is vulnerable at the start. But you "must" move.
      You cannot just "pass" - something has to happen every time. In an RPG - as Vincent puts it in AW, which captures this important advice - "there is no status quo".
      For the fiction, this is great. Status quo is bad - in movies, books, and RPGs... It is difficult to put in motion - while forcing players to take action, to have no "pass" option, is great.

      So the story (the game) starts balanced and with all pieces protected, but with each move, stuff happen.

      You move a piece in chess: you threaten another piece, you move to a better position, you control some squares, you try to get the center of the board for you and so on.
      By moving, you always "expose" something, you always take a risk. Actions can go bad, in RPGs, and this leads the fiction in the direction you didn't hope for... but that's what makes the game interesting.
      You move a piece forward, you take a risk, the configuration on the board is different - you take a risk to achieve something.

      I look at the pawns as the "fiction": they can only advance, they signal a commitment - you cannot move backwards; and I look at the major pieces as the players' capabilities to support that fiction - you need the major pieces backing up the advancing pawns, or your attempt to push the fiction will backfire...

      (and perhaps, more to follow..)

      About Producing Stories

      \nThere have been countless of debates around games and if they should be considered art or not. The Supreme Court of the United States stated in 2011 that games are to be considered art from a legal viewpoint. I would say that there are no finite answers about this controversial topic. Chris Bateman discusses over at his blog that games are art because they produce fiction.\n
      Every game tells a story. It may not be a good story - Pac-man's eternal quest to eat all the pac-dots and avoid the ghosts except when hopped up on power pills is hardly a candidate for adaptation to a book or film, but it is still a story.

      /.../

      Yet paintings also have rules to them \u2013 in the case of the Hay Wain it is a tacit rule of appreciating this and many other paintings that the paint on the canvas is a depiction of something, such that when we look at the Hay Wain we see a cart at the river, not a set of coloured paint splotches.

      /.../

      All representative art (which is most of, although not quite all, the things we consider art) involves this prescription to imagine.
      \nLets say that we assume that this is right, and it does makes sense if I think of games like chess or Monopoly. But what about poker? Perhaps the original background of why poker exist is lost? Perhaps we can create our own stories in retrospective to make this assumptions true\u2014that games are art because they produce, or is based around, fiction? We can glue on a story that the game that it's about the constant struggle of prospectors searching for gold.

      Because that's one way of understanding something: to put it in a context.

      If a game manual describes how a player would do something, it will be easier for the reader to understand the procedure if that fictive player's name is Hannah. It's easier to picture Hannah in a situation than a faceless character named "player".

      We take pride in that our hobby produces stories but I wonder if it's really that unique. The only thing that differs is the form in HOW we produce it. We got a form of interactivity (which is what decision-making is all about) that no other medium can achieve.

      Fiction-descriptive Mechanics
      If I take this one step further: if we create a story automatically from what we do in order to understand the task, couldn't we use this to create games that produces fiction? Some would probably object and say that we're already doing this. I'm saying that we're not using the right tools or mindset for it.

      When it comes to fortune-in-the-middle, where you 1) made all the calculations and modifications of the roll, 2) made the roll, and 3) used the result to describe what happened, there was a discussion around how the descriptions the player did never had any impact\u2014it was merely a flavor to add. If you rolled the dice and described the result, then it wouldn't matter if you shot someone in the head or kicked Hannah to the moon. What some people came up with was "...with teeth" to create a decision after the roll and make the fiction more valuable. This is the wrong mindset.

      What I propose is to create a distance between fiction and rules that must be filled with the players' descriptions. I propose that we can create a rule set that wont make any sense unless we put it into fiction. A rule set that is explained by putting it in a context. Very few roleplaying games do this today.

      There is a fiction going on in Monopoly that is described through the game mechanics. You buy estates and you use resources that looks like money. A similar fiction-descriptive mechanic, in roleplaying games, can be seen in skills. If you got Lockpick, the skill itself describes what will happen so the player doesn't need to fill it out with a description. This is what I mean when I say that we probably have the wrong mindset when it comes to designing roleplaying games that focuses on producing fiction. What we should put effort in is to create game mechanics that must be described in the fiction for them to make sense.

      If we want the players to produce the stories themselves, we should avoid fiction-descriptive mechanics.
      \n

      \nBernad Perron talks about this in the essay In the Frame of the Magic Cycle. That the game starts with the player knowing about the game and then uses that information to make a move. My own circle to reach immersion shows that the player knows information and makes the first move, but that person must get the information in the first place somehow. I dunno if it's good or bad if I assume that people understands this. I didn't bring it up because it can confuse anyone who reads about this.

      The Divided Brain

      \nIain McGilchrist talks about how the society focuses on the rational and mechanical when we should put as much energy on explaining the whole picture.



      I will come back to this topic, but this is what I feel most roleplaying game designers do: focusing on the smaller mechanical parts and make up procedures to fulfill them rather than making larger processes that contains them.

      \n If the mechanical parts responsible for content generation are suggestive rather than descriptive or prescriptive, and especially if randomization is employed, the game becomes a tool for imaginative association, a gateway to the unconscious; in short, a surrealist exercise.

      On the other hand, the same freedom opens up the very real possibility of becoming a Parody Engine.

      Bug or Feature?

      What is an Emotion?

      \nPaul Ekman (born February 15, 1934) is an American psychologist who is a pioneer in the study of emotions and their relation to facial expressions, has created an 'atlas of emotions' with more than ten thousand facial expressions, and has gained a reputation as "the best human lie detector in the world".
      \u2014Wikipedia

      Down below are three videos I think is worth watching and contemplating.



      We all share the same emotions but we react to them in different ways. We can seek out some of them and avoid others based on our preferences. Paul Ekman says that there are twelve different kinds of enjoyment. I'm personally not that interested in what emotion that the players experience. I'm not that interested in making a game \xbbfun\xab. I'm interested in why the game if fun. Not even Paul Ekman knows what an emotion really is. So this is a vague area, but what's not vague is the measures that can be used to invoke emotions.

      That's why I got the WHYs; the five broad categories Expression, Competition, Sensation, Exploration, and Destruction that tells us why someone would like to engage in our game. I'm not interested in describing them in great detail because they are such wide aspects that if I started to narrow them down, I would surely leave out some aspects of that WHY. These are more influences to awaken thought into the designers mind, when s/he combines them with the five WHATs: Meaning, Group, Structures, Fiction, and Setting. How would that look like?

      \n
      "The criteria for constructive emotional engagement is that it furthers collaboration and cooperation with that person, and as a benefit to a wider society." (towards the end of the video)
      \nBasically what I think roleplaying games, or all games, are about: creating interaction models. A "wider society" is, in my interpretation for this specific subject, the roleplaying group (but perhaps I'm wrong?).

      \n
      "It is the emotions that are built into us over the course of the evolution, because they were a benefit. If they weren't been used, we wouldn't have them." (50 sec into the video)
      \nWhen talking about flow and emotions above, I stated: "It's a constant cycle based on emotions. It's something built into our human psyche."\n
      "We need to understand the way we are the same, and we need to understand the way we are different." (5 min into the video)
      \nMihaly Csikszentmihalyi writes this about flow (Flow, Leadership and Joy in Work, p. 47): "To reach your potentials, which usually creates happiness, depends of two simultaneous processes. /.../ The first one is differentiations where one realize that we are unique individuals, responsible for our own survival and satisfaction, willing to develop our unique self wherever it may take us, meanwhile we enjoy how we express ourselves in action. The second process is about integration or the insight that no matter how unique we are, we're also totally encapsulated in a network of relations with other people, with cultural symbols and objects, and the surrounding environment."
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nRickard \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJanuary 2015 \n edited January 2015
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      Start With Why

      \n"People don't listen to WHAT you do but WHY you do it", is Simon Sinek's call for how to make people interested in what you want to produce. Start With Why is a book about marketing\u2014about selling ideas\u2014by using the basic foundation of how the human psyche works.\n
      No matter your definition of success /.../ how we go about achieving our goals is very similar. Some of us just wing it, but most of us try to at least gather some data so we can make educated decisions. /.../ Whatever the result, we make decisions based on a perception of the world that may not, in fact, be completely accurate.

      /.../

      There are times in which we had no data or we chose to ignore the advice or information at hand and just went with our gut and things worked out just fine, sometimes even better than expected. This dance between gut and rational decision-making pretty much covers how we conduct business and even live our lives.
      p. 12, 14
      \n
      The newest area of the brain, our Homo sapien brain, is the neocortex, which corresponds with the WHAT level. The neocortex is responsible for rational and analytical thought and language.

      The middle two sections comprise the limbic brain. The limbic brain is responsible for all our feelings, such as trust and loyalty. It is also responsible for all human behavior and all our decision-making, but it has no capacity for language.

      When we communicate from the outside in, when we communicate WHAT we do first, yes, people can understand vast amount of complicated information, like facts and features, but it does not drive behavior. But when we communicate from the inside out, we're talking directly to the part of the brain that controls decision-making, and our language part of the brain allows us to rationalize those decisions.

      The part of the brain that controls our feelings has no capacity for language. It is this disconnection that makes putting our feelings into words so hard.p. 55-56
      \nWHAT you do is pretty established.

      HOW you should do it is also easy to understand.

      WHY you do it is harder to explain. It's the WHY that makes people interested. It's what drives our decision-making.



      The brain is a complex thing and simply point at areas a say that "this part is responsible for [this] and that part for [that]" is always a gross simplification. But why I bring this up is because the emotions are part of why we make a decision. I'm currently reading (title translated from Swedish) Emotions and Convictions - how feelings affects our thoughts and one thing that stood out was that if we perceive a person doing remarkable deeds, we will start thinking of that person as remarkable. This might seem obvious, but here is the same thing in a different light (my interpretation): we like being acknowledged for our deeds. Getting praise or having someone use what you created is being acknowledged. Being acknowledged is enjoyable, and by getting enjoyable feedback related to the task, we think of the task we did to get that response as enjoyable.

      This is why I talk so much about interaction cycles, or feedback loops, as I also have called them. Another way of feeling enjoyment is by learning from the task. To later be able to master the task. A complex task is enjoyable because you can learn to decipher it. Games that are easy to understand becomes boring more quickly because the feedback loop will die out due to the fact that you wont learn anything new from the game after a while.\n
      The power of the limbic brain is astounding. It not only controls our gut decisions, but it can influence us to do things that seem illogical or irrational. /.../ It's not logic or facts but our hopes and dreams, our heart and our guts, that drives us to try new things.
      p. 62
      \nTo be able to understand a whole picture, you must know what you're fighting for. Knowing WHY is also a chance for you to bound with the product; to show others how you are as an individual by playing that game. In the gaming community, we look at a Vampire player in a different way than someone who is fond of Sorcerer, because their games expresses different WHYs despite both saying that they are focusing on \xbbstorytelling\xab.

      It might seem like I'm only talking about a product and making people buy that certain product, but the same thing goes with a rule system and explaining it. Begin with the WHY, follow up with HOW you play, and end with WHAT you use to play with. This goes for an overall arc as well as in individual chapters of the roleplaying book.

      Board game manuals are excellent at this. Read any board game manual, and you will see that the rule explanation begins with how you win the game. Board games has an advantage, because in almost all board games there is a competitive component. The designer doesn't need to explain WHY to play the game, because that is assumed as soon as you pick up that type of game. The only thing necessary is to explain the WHY within the gameplay - how a player can win. Tabletop roleplaying games doesn't have this luxury because we got several ways of why we want to play it, and there are also seldom a win, or end, condition. Perhaps the latter is something we should be better at incorporating?\n
      In nearly every case of a person or an organization that has gone on to inspire people and do great things, there exist this special partnership between WHY and HOW. Bill Gates, for example, may have been the visionary who imagined a world with a PC on every desk, but Paul Allen built the company. /.../

      This relationship starts to clarify the difference between a vision statement and a mission statement in an organization. The vision is the public statement of the founder's intent, WHY the company exist /.../ The mission statement is a description of the route,
      p. 142
      \nBy the way: I need someone who takes care of the marketing and distribution for This is Pulp.

      But again, if I compare this to a rule system. The WHY gives the broad perspective of what we're trying to accomplish. The HOW is the structure of play / gameplay. Very few roleplaying games explains either. They only focuses on WHAT, like character generation and dice rolling. The only thing that somewhat explains the HOW is adventure creation. I miss a clear structure of play in 98 % of all roleplaying games. You can probably find it in a majority of roleplaying games, but you have to cut and paste something together by analyzing different parts of the game text.

      This is why I think Forge games were so hard to understand at the beginning: the designers were used to how traditional roleplaying games were written and imitated that, and because the structure of play were missing in those games, the new storytelling games lacked it as well. That's why I have to read obscure blog posts to be able to play In a Wicked Age properly, and why there sometimes pops up threads on this forum that asks how a game should be played.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nRickard \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJanuary 2015 \n edited January 2015
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \nEmotions Steer our Perception
      Emotions and Convictions - how feelings affect our thoughts is a collection of essays about ... well, what the title says. The following is quoted from Convictions Through Emotions by Nico Frijda and Batja Mequita. It's translated to Swedish and it's me who translated the text to English.\n
      Conceptions are part of emotions. They are part of the emotional experiences because they are the meaning that are connected to the event. /.../ These conceptions form, together with the accompanying emotions of anger and enjoyment, a lot of the person's emotionally experiences.

      In such experience the conception is projected on the object or event as an attribute belonging to the object/event. One perceive wonderful and beautiful people, not people who awaken emotions of enjoyment and happiness. Sacred words are heard and read as reverend, and doesn't pass for words that give reverend. /.../ We also feel that prophets and political leaders evoke admiration because they are admirable, and not the other way around.
      p. 76
      \nSo a game is fun because it awakens the emotions of "fun". How are these emotions created? Theory of Fun says it comes from learning. Flow state from skill mastery. Uncertainty in Games from anticipating but never be sure of what to expect. Understanding Comics that it comes from expressing yourself, finding discoveries, and exercising the mind and body. Other sources, can't recall which ones now, say it comes from understanding complex things, like puzzles or emergent behavior. One category of Bartley's player types wants to beat others.

      I say that they are all right, but from different perspectives. Some are right in some cases, others are more broad and fit on everything, some are categories the other suggestions.\n
      Conceptions are the result of an assessment processes which either include activation of already existing conceptions relevant for the event, or creating new conceptionsabout the meaning of the event. In other words, the perception is part of the core of the emotion that sometimes have been created long before. To ride the city bus without a ticket and see the conductor will activate the already excising perception that you now will loose its face as well as money. /.../

      These perceptions that have been created on the spot are basically transient. They are created in the moment as a consequence of how an event is assessed. They are part of the emotional response. You get mad on the conductor because you feel that you are caught. /.../ If you are in love, you think the object for the love is beautiful and attractive. /.../ During a matrimonial argument, you can start question what you once saw in your spouse. But these perceptions are often transient and last only as long as the emotion. When the emotion is over, the perception fades. The experience of beauty and attraction usually goes away when love fades. The same mechanic can make you, after an intercourse, to vainly find the attractiveness in the partner that you had when you felt lust. /.../ After an argument you can be astounded by the evil words you thought and said to your spouse.
      p. 77
      \nThis brings me back to what @davidepignedoli said earlier and my response to him.\n
      Another example: all pieces start as "protected": all the pawns are protected by major pieces, and all major pieces are behind pawns, so that nothing is vulnerable at the start.
      \n
      Bernad Perron talks about this in the essay In the Frame of the Magic Cycle. That the game starts with the player knowing about the game and then uses that information to make a move.
      \nIt also makes me think about Huizinga\u2019s magic circle, where gameplay forms a protected circle, and things that happen in that circle stays in that circle. You can be vicious opponents trying to kill each other in the game but, when the game ends, you go back to be the childhood friends that you have always been.

      An emotional response is built up by anticipation and it normally fades quickly after the event, and leave reflection over what has happened. A reflected of WHAT and HOW it happened "tainted" by the emotion. Why do we want dice in roleplaying games? Is it because it's "fun to roll dice" or lies the answer somewhere else, not affected by the emotion triggered by the dice rolling?\n
      The conceptions that are built up within the emotions can go further than being emotional impressions (beauty, discomfort, frighting, big, fault) to transit to perceptions about real attributes. The conceptions can even become the foundation for development of the awaken emotion. /.../ Constructions of emotions can be even more far-reaching.

      Many perceptions have been created to fill out the image of an emotional situation. Other have been built up as justifications or explanations of the emotional assessment. All pain or discomfort may lead to blaming someone or something. [banging the head in a shelf makes you angry on the shelf. From an earlier example.] /.../

      Of these developments of perceptions, it's clear that emotions often aren't immediate responses on previous perceptions or conceptions. They are usually processes over an amount of time, part of cycles of information that creates emotional responses that creates new information, and so on.
      p. 77-78
      \nWhen talking about the major dramatic curve and how it was related to flow, I made this bold statement: "It's a constant cycle based on emotions. It's something built into our human psyche."

      I made that statement because of what I read in the quote above. We still don't know why it escalates, and I have earlier said that escalation is part of creating uncertainty, and uncertainty is what makes us curious. We don't know why, but it's there.

      One thesis is that the escalation is based on how the brain stores information with nodes and relations between the nodes. Whenever information is created, a node is created that with relations to other nodes, and the more that information is used, the larger the relations become. It still don't explain what an emotion is; all we noticed is that they are responses to something we learned/perceived.
      \n

      \n In one of the first posts in this thread, I talked about what a system consist of. In roleplaying games, we use "rule system" as a term for \xbba collection of rules\xab but I would like to point to another usage of the word "system" to help forming a game designer's mind; to help set a direction at the very start of creating a game. This time, I will use the words of Eric Zimmerman from The Video Game Theory Reader 2 in his essay Gaming Literacy: Game Design as a Model for Literacy in the Twenty-First Century.\n
      Systems
      To paraphrase contemporary communication theory, a system is a set of parts that interrelates to form a whole. Almost anything can be considered a system, from biological and physical systems to social and cultural systems. Having a systems point of view /.../ means understanding the world as dynamic sets of parts with complex, constantly changing interrelationships\u2014seeing the structures that underlie our world, and comprehending how these structures function. /.../

      System-based thinking is about process, not answers. It stresses the importance of dynamic relationships, not fixed facts. Getting to know a system requires understanding it on several levels, ...
      p. 25
      \nI will extract some parts from the quote:

      a set of parts that interrelates to form a whole

      dynamic

      changing

      process

      Emergence is the result of several parts interacting. Emergence is hard to predict, which makes it interesting. What intrigues us with the system is the dynamic that is created. Jim Butcher talks about the aftereffects of conflict in stories, and how they really are the interesting part because they are the consequences of the conflict. The conflict brings uncertainty but it's the consequences that create the feeling of change. Change is what keeps up the interest in the long run. We need to understand that what we create, in a game, is an ever-changing process, consisting of these parts that interacts with each other, instead of a collection of individual statements which roleplaying game mechanics have a habit to turn into.

      I will talk more about processes when I find a decent video with Edward de Bono.
      \n

      The Designer's Goal Should Be Part of the Participants' Interaction

      \nIn the game Svart av kval, vit av lust (Black of Despair, White of Lust), participants play intriguing vampires with their own personal agendas. The conflict resolution system is based on a hierarchy. A vampire hunter will always win over the elderly vampire, whom will always win over the younger vampire. At any conflict, the vampire can unleash The Beast within, and The Beast is on top of the hierarchy.\n
      The Beast > Elderly vampire > Younger vampire
      \nDuring the course of the game, other participants (it's a GM-free game) can impose The Beast's impulses; to feed, to be violent, to have sex, to humiliate the human side of the vampire. If the player wants to resist, that player will get a token. During conflict, if The Beast is released, any participant can take a token and tell the one who unleashed The Beast that the animal refusing helping. A refusing Beast is at the lowest point on the hierarchy.\n
      The Beast > Elderly vampire > Younger vampire > Refusing beast
      \nWhile playing the game, the elders can do whatever they want with the younger vampires, unless the younger ones use their Beasts. This mean that they must follow The Beast's impulses in any scene before that, to avoid having the Beast refusing helping out. The game is set up so the vampires will be tested through the impulses first, and then the fiction escalates to be about the vampires' goals.

      What the game is designed for is so that the younger vampires must walk on a path of destruction (to overcome the elderly vampires) if they want to achieve their goals. In other words, the game designer's goal is NOT the same thing as the participants' goals, but is instead part of the interaction between the players or the game components.


      (I really hope you can see the picture above)

      This might sound obvious, but what I feel is the opposite to this is design-through-rewards, where you get a tangible/extrinsic reward for doing a certain task. "Gain 1 XP if you walk on the path of destruction". Luke Crane is the perfect example of someone who designs games like this, and I can't really stress enough how bad that kind of design is, where the game designer's goal is the same thing as the participants.

      Why?

      Because extrinsic rewards doesn't have anything to do with the actual task.
      - The feedback from the task, the tangible reward, is not related in any way to what the player is doing.
      - The non-related feedback can steer away the attention to what the activity is about, and instead only be about the reward.
      - Scientific studies have shown that focusing on the outcome (the reward in this case), instead of the steps to achieve it, will decrease the intrinsic motivation.

      Is it good game design if the players do what you want them to do, without having to concern about if they think the activity is fun in the first place? (The sad thing is that this is a widely accepted game design philosophy when it comes to roleplaying games.)

      I prefer when a) what the game designer wants the game to be about is a natural part of the process of the game (the actual interaction), and b) when the feedback is related to the task. I will talk more about why a) and b) is important in the next posts.

      \n Again I want to thank you for you useful insights.

      I have a question regarding the picture in your post, where the designer goal meets the players interaction. Do you think this the same (or somehow similar) to the principles discussed by Vincent Baker for example, where he says that as a designer one should first of all think of which conversation he wants players to have at the table?
      In other words, if I design a game, my goal as the designer is not for example to encourage the player playing the thief to be dishonest, but rather to provide the player with the thief character certain interesting options, which would reasonably spring some conversation lines from his side that would lead to a 'dishonest' character..
      Am I saying this correctly?

      Let me try again.
      Bad Design for a thief character: when you steal gold coins from the treasure, at the expense of your companions, mark 1 XP
      This is the 'bad' design you're talking about, correct? Where as designer I hand you over an XP (or whatever reward) for doing something with your character.

      But how does the better design look like?

      Let me try (let's stay in the classic D&D/OSR style with a thief and a fighter):
      - what if I make a thief playbook which has the option to steal efficiently
      - AND that will level-up every X gold coins taken from treasures or companions
      - AND make each level-up harder to reach for the thief (thus the thief needs more gold than what his reasonable share would provide)
      does it achieve, you think, a better result?

      What if my dungeons all give treasures using odd or prime numbers like 7 gold coins, 11 gold coins? (that's hard to split in an even way)

      Still in the same game, if I make a fighter character and say "when you deal harm to one of your companions, mark XP" that's bad design.

      But if make a move like: when you fight, you deal 1D6-harm; if you have "blind-fury" deal 2D6-harm, but also 1-harm to companions next to you.
      And then: you enter blind-fury when a) you're Wounded or Critically Wounded, b) they've insulted your mum, c) you've harmed a companion in your previous fight, is this better?
      (plus once the fighter enters 'blind-fury' mode, he always harms companions if they stay close to him in a fight, thus triggers the move for the next fight over and over)

      I find your posts extremely useful and interesting, but I find myself falling back into the "do this and take 1 XP" design more than I'd like to.
      I think this design ("do this and take 1 XP") is not absolutely bad - when it deals with traditional players it is pushing them to explore different things and does it 'openly' and in a very 'honest' way: you read the character sheet and you know where it's going to take you.

      But I feel like it's a very 'lazy' design (without meaning to insult anyone) and leave very little space for surprises: I find it beautiful when I pick a character and little by little it takes me to places I didn't expect - and places that I am maybe even a bit uncomfortable with...
      This cannot happen with the "do this and take 1 XP".

      \n I'm too falling back on using design through (tangible) rewards, and it's because I've been taught that from other roleplaying games. It sounds true what Vincent Baker is saying about how the game is a conversation and that the game designer should decide what it should be about. The principle is simple: create emergence, meaning different parts interacting with each other. The chess pieces on the board are different parts that will interact with each other, but you need the parts to adopt their interaction with several other parts.

      We had a really cool thing happening in Mouse Guard once, where the group met a snake. Kenzie had an insight that said "Always consult Saxon when trouble arises.", and when the player of Saxon looked at his character sheet, the insight said "Always draw my sword at the first sign of trouble.". That was a great dependency but the trouble it is only an dependency to one and only one part. If this would happen again, the same thing would arise.

      The board game Pandemic has characters with unique powers. The medic can, for example decrease the presence of a decease from a town and, if they got the cure, remove it entirely. Another character can move other characters in its turn, meaning that the medic can move over great distances. A third character can reuse action cards, which can be combined with other powers. All these can work together in different ways, and will do that because of all the different situations that will occur on the game board.

      That's one way of designing interaction. Another way is to require something from the players. If they got a skill called Convince, one requirement could be to always use the sword (like Saxon), but another character must use money instead. So every time they try to convince someone, they have to include that requirement. You can expand this to have "violence", "sex", "money" as requirements, and then have skills like Convince, Finding Rumors, and Acquiring Items. Each one will have their own consequence in the game world of how the character is using it. Is Convincing + Sex appropriate for the prince or should he use Violence instead? The Money will end sooner or later.

      (To make it more complicated.) Do note that I'm not always against extrinsic motivations like tangible rewards (XP, loot, bonuses) but it needs to give a permanent effect and also feed back to the activity so it forms a loop. Slaying monster -> getting XP -> getting better -> slaying bigger monsters, is a perfectly fine loop to me. Slaying monsters -> getting XP -> becoming more convincing, is instead a broken loop.

      \n (Copy pasted from my G+ page where I talk about game design.)

      \n

      The Pitch is an Important Part of Your Game

      \nNicole Lazarro is using her four keys of fun - people fun, hard fun, easy fun, and serious fun - to broaden games to attract more people. Are your game lacking the social bit? Try to implement more of that to appeal to a larger audience. This is good and all if you want to think commercially, mostly if you're in the AAA business ... or, in the tabletop roleplaying world, making Dungeons and Dragons.

      I don't believe in making games that appeal to everyone, because it's my opinion that that kind of thinking is watering down the experience for all users. Create something powerful for one group of players - it can be a beautiful game, a philosophical thought, a strategic experience - and focus on pulling in just the right players.

      Through the pitch of your game, and even the cover, you can create expectations from the players. You can make them adapt their way of thinking to easier understand the gameplay, or accept the premise of the game. A rouge-like game, or a storytelling game, is very different from an educational game or a traditional roleplaying game.

      I would even go so far to say that the pitch is a part of your game system, because of the impact it will have on your players (that is, if they haven't got the hands on the game through other sources than you, like friends' recommendations or reading reviews). To write a really good pitch, you need to create curiosity so that the reader wants to know more, and you want to make it appealing for your target audience and make people realize what kind of game it is.

      I had people that didn't like the pitch for one of my games because they didn't like that kind of game style. That was a great feedback for me, because I don't want everyone to play that game. I want the players with a particular playstyle to play it.

      If you want advice to how to think when creating a pitch, check out the following article and video. Both are about leaving the mechanical behind (WHAT) and focus more on WHY to play the game.

      http://www.gamasutra.com/blogs/PeterAngstadt/20150203/235644/How_to_Make_Your_Pitch_Resonate.php

      http://www.ted.com/talks/simon_sinek_how_great_leaders_inspire_action
